<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">History of medicine</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the history of human medicine. For the book, see <a href="/wiki/A_History_of_Medicine" title="A History of Medicine"><i>A History of Medicine</i></a>.</div>

width="86"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><b>History of medicine</b>. From top-left to bottom-right: (<b>1</b>) a prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Trepanning" title="Trepanning">trepanted</a> skull of a woman (c. 3500 BCE), (<b>2</b>) a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">Byzantine</a> copy of the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> (c. 12th century CE), (<b>3</b>) <i>Studies of Embryos</i> by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> (1510-1513), (<b>4</b>) <i>The Bloodletting</i> by <a href="/wiki/Quirijn_van_Brekelenkam" title="Quirijn van Brekelenkam">Quirijn van Brekelenkam</a> (c. 1660), (<b>5</b>) a copy of Hua Shou's <i>Routes of the Fourteen Meridians and their Functions</i> (1716), (<b>6</b>) an operating theatre in <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> (1916), (<b>7</b>) <a href="/wiki/Joycelyn_Elders" title="Joycelyn Elders">Joycelyn Elders</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">Black American</a> to serve as <a href="/wiki/Surgeon_General_of_the_United_States" title="Surgeon General of the United States">Surgeon General of the United States</a> (1993-1994), (<b>8</b>) <a href="/wiki/Joe_Biden" title="Joe Biden">President Joe Biden</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> visiting the <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a> vaccine research center (2021), (<b>9</b>) an example of a public outdoor <a href="/wiki/Automated_external_defibrillator" title="Automated external defibrillator">Automated External Defibrillator</a> (AED) (2022).</div></div></div></div><p>The <b>history of medicine</b> is a both a study of medicine throughout history as well as a multidisciplinary field of study that seeks to explore and understand medical practices, both past and present, throughout human societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paterson_1982_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paterson_1982-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>More than just <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, this field of study incorporates learnings from across disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_health_sciences" title="Outline of health sciences">health sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_science" title="Political science">politics</a> to better understand the institutions, practices, people, professions, and social systems that have influenced and shaped medicine throughout the ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paterson_1982_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paterson_1982-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a documentation of medicine over time, the history of medicine shows how societies have changed in their approach to illness and disease from ancient times to the present. Early medical traditions include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_medicine" title="Babylonian medicine">those of Babylon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_Medicine" title="Traditional Chinese Medicine">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine" title="Ancient Egyptian medicine">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">India</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> was written in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> in the 5th century BCE, and is a direct inspiration for oaths of office that physicians swear upon entry into the profession today. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, surgical practices inherited from the ancient masters were improved and then systematized in <a href="/wiki/Rogerius_(physician)" title="Rogerius (physician)">Rogerius's</a> <i>The Practice of Surgery</i>. Universities began systematic training of physicians around 1220 CE in Italy.
</p><p>Invention of the microscope was a consequence of improved understanding, during the Renaissance. Prior to the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humorism</a> (also known as humoralism) was thought to explain the cause of disease but it was gradually replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>, leading to effective treatments and even cures for many infectious diseases. Military doctors advanced the methods of trauma treatment and surgery.  Public health measures were developed especially in the 19th century as the rapid growth of cities required systematic sanitary measures. Advanced research centers opened in the early 20th century, often connected with major hospitals.  The mid-20th century was characterized by new biological treatments, such as antibiotics. These advancements, along with developments in chemistry, genetics, and <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_medicine" title="Modern medicine">modern medicine</a>. Medicine was heavily professionalized in the 20th century, and new careers opened to women as nurses (from the 1870s) and as physicians (especially after 1970).
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-2"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Prehistoric_medicine"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Prehistoric medicine</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_medicine">Prehistoric medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prehistoric medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_medicine" title="Prehistoric medicine">Prehistoric medicine</a></div>
<p>Although there is little record to establish when plants were first used for medicinal purposes (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbalist" title="Herbalist">herbalism</a>), the use of plants as healing agents, as well as clays and soils is ancient.  Over time, through emulation of the behavior of fauna, a medicinal knowledge base developed and passed between generations. Even earlier, Neanderthals may have engaged in medical practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
As tribal culture specialized specific castes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shaman" title="Shaman">shamans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apothecary" title="Apothecary">apothecaries</a> fulfilled the role of healer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
The first known <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a> dates to c. 7000 BCE in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluchistan" title="Baluchistan">Baluchistan</a> where Neolithic dentists used flint-tipped drills and bowstrings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The first known <a href="/wiki/Trepanning" title="Trepanning">trepanning</a> operation was carried out c. 5000 BCE in <a href="/wiki/Ensisheim" title="Ensisheim">Ensisheim</a>, France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> A possible <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">amputation</a> was carried out c. 4,900 BCE in Buthiers-Bulancourt, France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_medicine">Ancient medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Ancient China</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg/220px-Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg/330px-Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg/440px-Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traditional_Chinese_medicine_in_Xi%27an_market.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Assorted dried plant and animal parts used in traditional Chinese medicines, clockwise from top left corner: dried <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingzhi_mushroom" title="Lingzhi mushroom">Lingzhi</a> (lit. "spirit mushrooms"), <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siraitia_grosvenorii" title="Siraitia grosvenorii">Luo Han Guo</a>, turtle shell underbelly (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plastron" title="Plastron">plastron</a>), and dried curled snakes</div></div></div>
<p>China also developed a large body of traditional medicine. Much of the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">traditional Chinese medicine</a> derived from empirical observations of disease and illness by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taoist" title="Taoist">Taoist</a> physicians and reflects the classical Chinese belief that individual human experiences express causative principles effective in the environment at all scales. These causative principles, whether material, essential, or mystical, correlate as the expression of the natural order of the universe.
</p><p>The foundational text of Chinese medicine is the <i><a href="/wiki/Huangdi_Neijing" title="Huangdi Neijing">Huangdi neijing</a></i>, (or <i>Yellow Emperor's Inner Canon</i>), written 5th century to 3rd century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>  Near the end of the 2nd century CE, during the Han dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zhongjing" title="Zhang Zhongjing">Zhang Zhongjing</a>, wrote a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shang_Han_Lun" title="Shang Han Lun">Treatise on Cold Damage</a></i>, which contains the earliest known reference to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neijing_Suwen" title="Neijing Suwen">Neijing Suwen</a></i>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_Dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin Dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a> practitioner and advocate of <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moxibustion" title="Moxibustion">moxibustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huangfu_Mi" title="Huangfu Mi">Huangfu Mi</a> (215–282), also quotes the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a> in his <i>Jiayi jing</i>, c. 265.  During the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, the <i>Suwen</i> was expanded and revised and is now the best extant representation of the foundational roots of traditional Chinese medicine.  <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a> that is based on the use of herbal medicine, acupuncture, massage and other forms of therapy has been practiced in China for thousands of years.
</p><p>In the 18th century, during the Qing dynasty, there was a proliferation of popular books as well as more advanced encyclopedias on traditional medicine.  Jesuit missionaries introduced Western science and medicine to the royal court, although the Chinese physicians ignored them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Finally in the 19th century, Western medicine was introduced at the local level by Christian medical missionaries from the <a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">London Missionary Society</a> (Britain), the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=TMethodist_Church&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="TMethodist Church (page does not exist)">tMethodist Church</a> (Britain) and the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States" title="Presbyterian Church in the United States">Presbyterian Church (US)</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hobson" title="Benjamin Hobson">Benjamin Hobson</a> (1816–1873) in 1839, set up a highly successful Wai Ai Clinic in Guangzhou, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_College_of_Medicine_for_Chinese" title="Hong Kong College of Medicine for Chinese">Hong Kong College of Medicine for Chinese</a> was founded in 1887 by the <a href="/wiki/London_Missionary_Society" title="London Missionary Society">London Missionary Society</a>, with its first graduate (in 1892) being <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a>, who later led the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Revolution_(1911)" title="Chinese Revolution (1911)">Chinese Revolution (1911)</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_College_of_Medicine_for_Chinese" title="Hong Kong College of Medicine for Chinese">Hong Kong College of Medicine for Chinese</a> was the forerunner of the School of Medicine of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hong_Kong" title="University of Hong Kong">University of Hong Kong</a>, which started in 1911.
</p><p>Because of the social custom that men and women should not be near to one another, the women of China were reluctant to be treated by male doctors. The missionaries sent women doctors such as Dr. <a href="/wiki/Mary_Hannah_Fulton" title="Mary Hannah Fulton">Mary Hannah Fulton</a> (1854–1927). Supported by the Foreign Missions Board of the Presbyterian Church (US) she in 1902 founded the first medical college for women in China, the Hackett Medical College for Women, in Guangzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Historiography of Chinese medicine</b>
</p><p>When reading the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">Chinese classics</a>, it is important for scholars to examine these works from the Chinese perspective. Historians have noted two key aspects of Chinese medical history: understanding conceptual differences when translating the term 身, and observing the history from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> rather than biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19">: 19 </span></sup>
</p><p>In Chinese classical texts, the term 身 is the closest historical translation to the English word "body" because it sometimes refers to the physical human body in terms of being weighed or measured, but the term is to be understood as an "ensemble of functions" encompassing both the human psyche and emotions. This concept of the human body is opposed to the European duality of a separate mind and body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19–20">: 19–20 </span></sup> It is critical for scholars to understand the fundamental differences in concepts of the body in order to connect the medical theory of the classics to the "human organism" it is explaining.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 20">: 20 </span></sup>
</p><p>Chinese scholars established a correlation between the cosmos and the "human organism." The basic components of cosmology, qi, yin yang and the Five Phase theory, were used to explain health and disease in texts such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huangdi_neijing" title="Huangdi neijing">Huangdi neijing</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 21">: 21 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">Yin and yang</a> are the changing factors in cosmology, with <a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a> as the vital force or energy of life. The Five Phase theory <a href="/wiki/Wuxing_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Wuxing (Chinese philosophy)">Wu Xing</a> of the Han dynasty contains the elements wood, fire, earth, metal, and water. By understanding medicine from a cosmology perspective, historians better understand Chinese medical and social classifications such as gender, which was defined by a domination or remission of yang in terms of yin.
</p><p>These two distinctions are imperative when analyzing the history of traditional Chinese medical science.
</p><p>A majority of Chinese medical history written after the classical canons comes in the form of primary source case studies where academic physicians record the illness of a particular person and the healing techniques used, as well as their effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-4"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 225">: 225 </span></sup> Historians have noted that Chinese scholars wrote these studies instead of "books of prescriptions or advice manuals;" in their historical and environmental understanding, no two illnesses were alike so the healing strategies of the practitioner was unique every time to the specific diagnosis of the patient.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-5"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 226–27">: 226–27 </span></sup> Medical case studies existed throughout Chinese history, but "individually authored and published case history" was a prominent creation of the Ming dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-6"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 225">: 225 </span></sup> An example such case studies would be the literati physician, Cheng Congzhou, collection of 93 cases published in 1644.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furth_12-7"><a href="#cite_note-Furth-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 226–27">: 226–27 </span></sup>
</p>
<p><b>Ancient India</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unani" title="Unani">Unani</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siddha_medicine" title="Siddha medicine">Siddha medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="820" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg/170px-Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg/255px-Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg/340px-Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ayurvada_Medicine_from_Medicinal_Plants_YVSREDDY.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurvedic</a> herbal medicines</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atharvaveda" title="Atharvaveda">Atharvaveda</a>, a sacred text of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> dating from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Age_India" title="Iron Age India">Early Iron Age</a>, is one of the first Indian texts dealing with medicine. The Atharvaveda also contains prescriptions of herbs for various ailments. The use of herbs to treat ailments would later form a large part of <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a>.
</p><p>Ayurveda, meaning the "complete knowledge for long life" is another medical system of India. Its two most famous texts belong to the schools of <a href="/wiki/Charaka" title="Charaka">Charaka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta</a>. The earliest foundations of Ayurveda were built on a synthesis of traditional herbal practices together with a massive addition of theoretical conceptualizations, new <a href="/wiki/Nosology" title="Nosology">nosologies</a> and new therapies dating from about 600 BCE onwards, and coming out of the communities of thinkers which included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the compendium of <a href="/wiki/Charaka" title="Charaka">Charaka</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Charaka_Samhita" title="Charaka Samhita">Charakasamhitā</a>, health and disease are not predetermined and life may be prolonged by human effort. The compendium of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Suśruta</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sushruta_Samhita" title="Sushruta Samhita">Suśrutasamhitā</a> defines the purpose of medicine to cure the diseases of the sick, protect the healthy, and to prolong life. Both these ancient compendia include details of the examination, diagnosis, treatment, and prognosis of numerous ailments.  The <a href="/wiki/Sushruta_Samhita" title="Sushruta Samhita">Suśrutasamhitā</a> is notable for describing procedures on various forms of <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Rhinoplasty" title="Rhinoplasty">rhinoplasty</a>, the repair of torn ear lobes, perineal <a href="/wiki/Lithotomy" title="Lithotomy">lithotomy</a>, cataract surgery, and several other excisions and other surgical procedures. Most remarkable was Susruta's surgery specially the rhinoplasty for which he is called father of modern plastic surgery. Susruta also described more than 125 surgical instruments in detail. Also remarkable is Sushruta's penchant for scientific classification:
His medical treatise consists of 184 chapters, 1,120 conditions are listed, including injuries and illnesses relating to aging and mental illness.
</p><p>The Ayurvedic classics mention eight branches of medicine: kāyācikitsā (<a href="/wiki/Internal_medicine" title="Internal medicine">internal medicine</a>), śalyacikitsā (surgery including anatomy), śālākyacikitsā (eye, ear, nose, and throat diseases), kaumārabhṛtya (pediatrics with obstetrics and gynaecology), bhūtavidyā (spirit and psychiatric medicine), agada tantra (<a href="/wiki/Toxicology" title="Toxicology">toxicology</a> with treatments of stings and bites), rasāyana (science of rejuvenation), and vājīkaraṇa (<a href="/wiki/Aphrodisiac" title="Aphrodisiac">aphrodisiac</a> and fertility). Apart from learning these, the student of Āyurveda was expected to know ten arts that were indispensable in the preparation and application of his medicines: distillation, operative skills, cooking, horticulture, metallurgy, sugar manufacture, pharmacy, analysis and separation of minerals, compounding of metals, and preparation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkalis" title="Alkalis">alkalis</a>.  The teaching of various subjects was done during the instruction of relevant clinical subjects.  For example, the teaching of anatomy was a part of the teaching of surgery, embryology was a part of training in pediatrics and obstetrics, and the knowledge of physiology and pathology was interwoven in the teaching of all the clinical disciplines.
The normal length of the student's training appears to have been seven years.  But the physician was to continue to learn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>As an alternative form of medicine in India, <a href="/wiki/Unani_medicine" title="Unani medicine">Unani medicine</a> found deep roots and royal patronage during medieval times. It progressed during the Indian sultanate and <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">mughal</a> periods. Unani medicine is very close to Ayurveda. Both are based on the theory of the presence of the elements (in Unani, they are considered to be fire, water, earth, and air) in the human body. According to followers of Unani medicine, these elements are present in different fluids and their balance leads to health and their imbalance leads to illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 18th century CE, Sanskrit medical wisdom still dominated. Muslim rulers built large hospitals in 1595 in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyderabad,_India" title="Hyderabad, India">Hyderabad</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Delhi" title="History of Delhi">Delhi</a> in 1719, and numerous commentaries on ancient texts were written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deepak_Kumar_2003_pp_680-3_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deepak_Kumar_2003_pp_680-3-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Africa.2FMiddle_East"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa/Middle_East">Africa/Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Africa/Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_2">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Ancient Egypt</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_medicine" title="Ancient Egyptian medicine">Ancient Egyptian medicine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:183px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hieroglyphic-brain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="97" data-file-width="181" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Hieroglyphic-brain.jpg" width="181"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hieroglyphic-brain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwin_Smith_Surgical_Papyrus" title="Edwin Smith Surgical Papyrus">Edwin Smith Surgical Papyrus</a>, written in the 17th century BCE, contains the earliest recorded reference to the brain. <a href="/wiki/New_York_Academy_of_Medicine" title="New York Academy of Medicine">New York Academy of Medicine</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> developed a large, varied and fruitful medical tradition. <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> described the Egyptians as "the healthiest of all men, next to the Libyans",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> because of the dry climate and the notable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_health_system" title="Public health system">public health system</a> that they possessed. According to him, "the practice of medicine is so specialized among them that each physician is a healer of one disease and no more."  Although Egyptian medicine, to a considerable extent, dealt with the supernatural,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> it eventually developed a practical use in the fields of anatomy, public health, and clinical diagnostics.
</p><p>Medical information in the <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus" title="Edwin Smith Papyrus">Edwin Smith Papyrus</a> may date to a time as early as 3000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Imhotep" title="Imhotep">Imhotep</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Third dynasty of Egypt">3rd dynasty</a> is sometimes credited with being the founder of ancient Egyptian medicine and with being the original author of the <i>Edwin Smith Papyrus</i>, detailing cures, ailments and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomical" title="Anatomical">anatomical</a> observations. The <i>Edwin Smith Papyrus</i> is regarded as a copy of several earlier works and was written c. 1600 BCE. It is an ancient textbook on surgery almost completely devoid of magical thinking and describes in exquisite detail the <i>examination, diagnosis, treatment,</i> and <i>prognosis</i> of numerous ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The  <a href="/wiki/Kahun_Gynaecological_Papyrus" title="Kahun Gynaecological Papyrus">Kahun Gynaecological Papyrus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> treats women's complaints, including problems with conception. Thirty four cases detailing diagnosis and<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bynum_2006_p198–199_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bynum_2006_p198–199-22">[22]</a></sup>  treatment survive, some of them fragmentarily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Dating to 1800 BCE, it is the oldest surviving medical text of any kind.
</p><p>Medical institutions, referred to as Houses of Life are known to have been established in ancient Egypt as early as 2200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a> is the oldest written text mentioning <a href="/wiki/Enema" title="Enema">enemas</a>. Many medications were administered by enemas and one of the many types of medical specialists was an Iri, the Shepherd of the Anus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest known physician is also credited to <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Egypt" title="History of ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hesy-Ra" title="Hesy-Ra">Hesy-Ra</a>, "Chief of Dentists and Physicians" for King <a href="/wiki/Djoser" title="Djoser">Djoser</a> in the 27th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Also, the earliest known woman physician, <a href="/wiki/Peseshet" title="Peseshet">Peseshet</a>, practiced in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> at the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fourth dynasty of Egypt">4th dynasty</a>. Her title was "Lady Overseer of the Lady Physicians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><b>Mesopotamia</b></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet,_from_Nippur,_Iraq,_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum,_Istanbul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4999" data-file-width="3379" decoding="async" height="325" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/220px-Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/330px-Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg/440px-Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet%2C_from_Nippur%2C_Iraq%2C_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum%2C_Istanbul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medical_recipe_concerning_poisoning._Terracotta_tablet,_from_Nippur,_Iraq,_18th_century_BCE._Ancient_Orient_Museum,_Istanbul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medical recipe concerning poisoning. Terracotta tablet, from Nippur, Iraq, 18th century BCE. Ancient Orient Museum, Istanbul</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2245" data-file-width="1017" decoding="async" height="397" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg/180px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg/270px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg/360px-Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustrerad_Verldshistoria_band_I_Ill_032.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monumental stone relief of a fish-garbed figure from the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Ninurta" title="Ninurta">Ninurta</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalhu" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a>, believed by some experts to be a representation of an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asipu" title="Asipu">āšipu</a></i>, or exorcist-priest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 83">: 83 </span></sup> who functioned as a kind of healer and primitive doctor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_medicine" title="Babylonian medicine">Babylonian medicine</a></div>
<p>The ancient Mesopotamians had no distinction between "rational science" and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farber1995_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farber1995-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abusch_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abusch-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">[32]</a></sup> When a person became ill, doctors would prescribe both magical formulas to be recited as well as medicinal treatments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farber1995_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farber1995-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abusch_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abusch-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup> The earliest medical prescriptions appear in <a href="/wiki/Sumerian_language" title="Sumerian language">Sumerian</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Third_Dynasty_of_Ur" title="Third Dynasty of Ur">Third Dynasty of Ur</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2112 BCE – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 2004 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The oldest Babylonian texts on medicine date back to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Babylonian_dynasty" title="First Babylonian dynasty">Old Babylonian</a> period in the first half of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BCE" title="2nd millennium BCE">2nd millennium BCE</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The most extensive Babylonian medical text, however, is the <i>Diagnostic Handbook</i> written by the <i>ummânū</i>, or chief scholar, <a href="/wiki/Esagil-kin-apli" title="Esagil-kin-apli">Esagil-kin-apli</a> of <a href="/wiki/Borsippa" title="Borsippa">Borsippa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horstmanshoff_2004_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horstmanshoff_2004-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 99">: 99 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heeßel_2004_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heeßel_2004-36">[36]</a></sup> during the reign of the Babylonian king <a href="/wiki/Adad-apla-iddina" title="Adad-apla-iddina">Adad-apla-iddina</a> (1069–1046 BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Along with the Egyptians, the Babylonians introduced the practice of diagnosis, prognosis, physical examination, and remedies. In addition, the <i>Diagnostic Handbook</i> introduced the methods of <a href="/wiki/Therapy" title="Therapy">therapy</a> and cause. The text contains a list of medical symptoms and often detailed empirical observations along with logical rules used in combining observed symptoms on the body of a patient with its diagnosis and prognosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horstmanshoff_2004_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horstmanshoff_2004-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 97–98">: 97–98 </span></sup> The <i>Diagnostic Handbook</i> was based on a logical set of axioms and assumptions, including the modern view that through the examination and inspection of the symptoms of a patient, it is possible to determine the patient's disease, its cause and future development, and the chances of the patient's recovery. The symptoms and diseases of a patient were treated through therapeutic means such as bandages, herbs and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cream_(pharmaceutical)" title="Cream (pharmaceutical)">creams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horstmanshoff_2004_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Horstmanshoff_2004-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 99">: 99 </span></sup>
</p><p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Semitic" title="East Semitic">East Semitic</a> cultures, the main medicinal authority was a kind of exorcist-healer known as an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asipu" title="Asipu">āšipu</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farber1995_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Farber1995-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abusch_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abusch-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-32">[32]</a></sup> The profession was generally passed down from father to son<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farber1995_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Farber1995-30">[30]</a></sup> and was held in extremely high regard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farber1995_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Farber1995-30">[30]</a></sup> Of less frequent recourse was another kind of healer known as an <i>asu</i>, who corresponds more closely to a modern physician<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup> and treated physical symptoms using primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_remedies" title="Folk remedies">folk remedies</a> composed of various herbs, animal products, and minerals, as well as potions, enemas, and ointments or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poultices" title="Poultices">poultices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup> These physicians, who could be either male or female, also dressed wounds, set limbs, and performed simple surgeries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup> The ancient Mesopotamians also practiced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophylaxis" title="Prophylaxis">prophylaxis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup> and took measures to prevent the spread of disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McIntosh2005_29-6"><a href="#cite_note-McIntosh2005-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mental illnesses were well known in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup> where diseases and mental disorders were believed to be caused by specific deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup> Because <a href="/wiki/Hand" title="Hand">hands</a> symbolized control over a person, mental illnesses were known as "hands" of certain deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup> One psychological illness was known as <i>Qāt Ištar</i>, meaning "Hand of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ishtar" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup> Others were known as "Hand of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a>", "Hand of the Ghost", and "Hand of the God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup> Descriptions of these illnesses, however, are so vague that it is usually impossible to determine which illnesses they correspond to in modern terminology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlackGreen1992_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-BlackGreen1992-28">[28]</a></sup> Mesopotamian doctors kept detailed record of their patients' <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a> and assigned spiritual meanings to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup> A patient who hallucinated that he was seeing a dog was predicted to die;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup> whereas, if he saw a gazelle, he would recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup> The royal family of <a href="/wiki/Elam" title="Elam">Elam</a> was notorious for its members frequently suffering from insanity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erectile_dysfunction" title="Erectile dysfunction">Erectile dysfunction</a> was recognized as being rooted in psychological problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NemetNejat_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-NemetNejat-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas">Ideas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ideas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Humors</b></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1277" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg/170px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg/255px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg/340px-13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:13th_century_anatomical_illustration_-_sharp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>13th-century illustration showing the veins. <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>, Oxford.</div></div></div>
<p>The theory of humors was derived from ancient medical works, dominated western medicine until the 19th century, and is credited to Greek philosopher and surgeon Galen of Pergamon (129–<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 216 BCE</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Syros_2013_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Syros_2013-39">[39]</a></sup> In Greek medicine, there are thought to be four humors, or bodily fluids that are linked to illness: blood, phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> Early scientists believed that food is digested into blood, muscle, and bones, while the humors that weren't blood were then formed by  indigestible materials that are left over. An excess or shortage of any one of the four humors is theorized to cause an imbalance that results in sickness; the aforementioned statement was hypothesized by sources before Hippocrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> Hippocrates (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 400 BCE</span>) deduced that the four seasons of the year and four ages of man that affect the body in relation to the humors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Syros_2013_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Syros_2013-39">[39]</a></sup> The four ages of man are childhood, youth, prime age, and old age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> The four humors as associated with the four seasons are black bile – autumn, yellow bile – summer, phlegm – winter and blood – spring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>De temperamentis,</i> Galen linked what he called temperaments, or personality characteristics, to a person's natural mixture of humors. He also said that the best place to check the balance of temperaments was in the palm of the hand. A person that is considered to be phlegmatic is said to be an introvert, even-tempered, calm, and peaceful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> This person would have an excess of phlegm, which is described as a viscous substance or mucous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Similarly, a melancholic temperament related to being moody, anxious, depressed, introverted, and pessimistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> A melancholic temperament is caused by an excess of black bile, which is sedimentary and dark in color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Being extroverted, talkative, easygoing, carefree, and sociable coincides with a sanguine temperament, which is linked to too much blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> Finally, a choleric temperament is related to too much yellow bile, which is actually red in color and has the texture of foam; it is associated with being aggressive, excitable, impulsive, and also extroverted.
</p><p>There are numerous ways to treat a disproportion of the humors. For example, if someone was suspected to have too much blood, then the physician would perform bloodletting as a treatment. Likewise, if a person that had too much phlegm would feel better after expectorating, and someone with too much yellow bile would purge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Another factor to be considered in the balance of humors is the quality of air in which one resides, such as the climate and elevation. Also, the standard of food and drink, balance of sleeping and waking, exercise and rest, retention and evacuation are important. Moods such as anger, sadness, joy, and love can affect the balance. During that time, the importance of balance was demonstrated by the fact that women lose blood monthly during menstruation, and have a lesser occurrence of gout, arthritis, and epilepsy then men do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Galen also hypothesized that there are three faculties. The natural faculty affects growth and reproduction and is produced in the liver. Animal or vital faculty controls respiration and emotion, coming from the heart. In the brain, the psychic faculty commands the senses and thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> The structure of bodily functions is related to the humors as well. Greek physicians understood that food was cooked in the stomach; this is where the nutrients are extracted. The best, most potent and pure nutrients from food are reserved for blood, which is produced in the liver and carried through veins to organs. Blood enhanced with pneuma, which means wind or breath, is carried by the arteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-6"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> The path that blood take is as follows: venous blood passes through the vena cava and is moved into the right ventricle of the heart; then, the pulmonary artery takes it to the lungs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup> Later, the pulmonary vein then mixes air from the lungs with blood to form arterial blood, which has different observable characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stelmack_1991_40-7"><a href="#cite_note-Stelmack_1991-40">[40]</a></sup> After leaving the liver, half of the yellow bile that is produced travels to the blood, while the other half travels to the gallbladder. Similarly, half of the black bile produced gets mixed in with blood, and the other half is used by the spleen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pormann_2007_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pormann_2007-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_3">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Ancient Greek medicine</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_medicine" title="Ancient Greek medicine">Ancient Greek medicine</a></div>
<p>Around 800 BCE <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> gives descriptions of wound treatment by the two sons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asklepios" title="Asklepios">Asklepios</a>, the admirable physicians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Podaleirius" title="Podaleirius">Podaleirius</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machaon_(physician)" title="Machaon (physician)">Machaon</a> and one acting doctor, <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>. Because Machaon is wounded and Podaleirius is in combat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eurypylus_(king_of_Thessaly)" title="Eurypylus (king of Thessaly)">Eurypylus</a> asks Patroclus to "cut out the arrow-head, and wash the dark blood from my thigh with warm water, and sprinkle soothing herbs with power to heal on my wound".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Asklepios, like <a href="/wiki/Imhotep" title="Imhotep">Imhotep</a>, came to be associated as a god of healing over time.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kos_Asklepeion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="876" data-file-width="1453" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/260px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/390px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Kos_Asklepeion.jpg/520px-Kos_Asklepeion.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kos_Asklepeion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the <i>Askleipion</i> of <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a>, the best preserved instance of an Asklepieion.</div></div></div>
<p>Temples dedicated to the healer-god <a href="/wiki/Asclepius" title="Asclepius">Asclepius</a>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Asclepeion" title="Asclepeion">Asclepieia</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Ἀσκληπιεῖα</span>, sing. <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀσκληπιεῖον</span></span>, <i>Asclepieion</i>), functioned as centers of medical advice, prognosis, and healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-44">[44]</a></sup> At these shrines, patients would enter a dream-like state of induced sleep known as <i>enkoimesis</i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἐγκοίμησις</span></span>) not unlike anesthesia, in which they either received guidance from the deity <a href="/wiki/Sleep_temple" title="Sleep temple">in a dream</a> or were cured by surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Askitopoulou,_H._2002_p.11-17_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Askitopoulou,_H._2002_p.11-17-45">[45]</a></sup> Asclepeia provided carefully controlled spaces conducive to healing and fulfilled several of the requirements of institutions created for healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-44">[44]</a></sup>  In the Asclepeion of <a href="/wiki/Epidaurus" title="Epidaurus">Epidaurus</a>, three large marble boards dated to 350 BCE preserve the names, case histories, complaints, and cures of about 70 patients who came to the temple with a problem and shed it there.  Some of the surgical cures listed, such as the opening of an abdominal abscess or the removal of traumatic foreign material, are realistic enough to have taken place, but with the patient in a state of enkoimesis induced with the help of soporific substances such as opium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Askitopoulou,_H._2002_p.11-17_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Askitopoulou,_H._2002_p.11-17-45">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alcmaeon_of_Croton" title="Alcmaeon of Croton">Alcmaeon of Croton</a> wrote on medicine between 500 and 450 BCE. He argued that channels linked the sensory organs to the brain, and it is possible that he discovered one type of channel, the optic nerves, by dissection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kos" title="Kos">Kos</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 460</span> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 370 BCE</span>), considered the "father of modern medicine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a> is a collection of around seventy early medical works from ancient Greece strongly associated with Hippocrates and his students.  Most famously, the Hippocratics invented the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> for physicians. Contemporary physicians swear an oath of office which includes aspects found in early editions of the Hippocratic Oath.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> and his followers were first to describe many diseases and medical conditions. Though <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humorism</a> (humoralism) as a medical system predates 5th-century Greek medicine, Hippocrates and his students systematized the thinking that illness can be explained by an imbalance of blood, phlegm, black bile, and yellow bile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Hippocrates is given credit for the first description of <a href="/wiki/Nail_clubbing" title="Nail clubbing">clubbing</a> of the fingers, an important diagnostic sign in chronic suppurative lung disease, <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanotic_heart_defect" title="Cyanotic heart defect">cyanotic heart disease</a>. For this reason, clubbed fingers are sometimes referred to as "Hippocratic fingers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Hippocrates was also the first physician to describe the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippocratic_face" title="Hippocratic face">Hippocratic face</a> in <i>Prognosis</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> famously alludes to this description when writing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falstaff" title="Falstaff">Falstaff</a>'s death in Act II, Scene iii. of <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">Henry V</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Hippocrates began to categorize illnesses as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_(medical)" title="Acute (medical)">acute</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medicine)" title="Chronic (medicine)">chronic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">endemic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemic</a>, and use terms such as, "exacerbation, <a href="/wiki/Relapse" title="Relapse">relapse</a>, resolution, crisis, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paroxysm" title="Paroxysm">paroxysm</a>, peak, and <a href="/wiki/Convalescence" title="Convalescence">convalescence</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverberg1967_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Silverberg1967-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loudon2002_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loudon2002-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greek <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> (c. 129–216 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>) was one of the greatest physicians of the ancient world, as his theories dominated all medical studies for nearly 1500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hajar_2012_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hajar_2012-54">[54]</a></sup> His theories and experimentation laid the foundation for modern medicine surrounding the heart and blood. Galen's influence and innovations in medicine can be attributed to the experiments he conducted, which were unlike any other medical experiments of his time. Galen strongly believed that medical dissection was one of the essential procedures in truly understanding medicine. He began to dissect different animals that were anatomically similar to humans, which allowed him to learn more about the internal organs and extrapolate the surgical studies to the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hajar_2012_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hajar_2012-54">[54]</a></sup> In addition, he performed many audacious operations—including brain and eye surgeries—that were not tried again for almost two millennia. Through the dissections and surgical procedures, Galen concluded that blood is able to circulate throughout the human body, and the heart is most similar to the human soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hajar_2012_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hajar_2012-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21781247_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21781247-55">[55]</a></sup> In <i>Ars medica</i> ("Arts of Medicine"), he further explains the mental properties in terms of specific mixtures of the bodily organs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> While much of his work surrounded the physical anatomy, he also worked heavily in humoral physiology.
</p><p>Galen's medical work was regarded as authoritative until well into the Middle Ages. He left a physiological model of the human body that became the mainstay of the medieval physician's university anatomy curriculum. Although he attempted to extrapolate the animal dissections towards the model of the human body, some of Galen's theories were incorrect. This caused his model to suffer greatly from stasis and intellectual stagnation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Greek and Roman taboos caused dissection of the human body to usually be banned in ancient times, but in the Middle Ages it changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Numbers_2009_45_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Numbers_2009_45-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.harvard.edu_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.harvard.edu-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1523 Galen's <i>On the Natural Faculties</i> was published in London.  In the 1530s Belgian anatomist and physician <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> launched a project to translate many of Galen's Greek texts into Latin.  Vesalius's most famous work, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i> was greatly influenced by Galenic writing and form.
</p>
<p><b>Ancient Roman medicine</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_ancient_Rome" title="Medicine in ancient Rome">Medicine in ancient Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medical_community_of_ancient_Rome" title="Medical community of ancient Rome">Medical community of ancient Rome</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> invented numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_instruments" title="Surgical instruments">surgical instruments</a>, including the first instruments unique to women,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rome_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rome-61">[61]</a></sup> as well as the surgical uses of <a href="/wiki/Forceps" title="Forceps">forceps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scalpel" title="Scalpel">scalpels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cautery#History" title="Cautery">cautery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scissors" title="Scissors">cross-bladed scissors</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_needle" title="Surgical needle">surgical needle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sound_(medical_instrument)" title="Sound (medical instrument)">sound</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Speculum_(medical)" title="Speculum (medical)">speculas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Surgery_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Surgery-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirurgia_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chirurgia-63">[63]</a></sup> Romans also performed <a href="/wiki/Cataract_surgery" title="Cataract surgery">cataract surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Roman army physician <a href="/wiki/Pedanius_Dioscorides" title="Pedanius Dioscorides">Dioscorides</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 40</span>–90 CE), was a Greek botanist and pharmacologist. He wrote the encyclopedia <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Materia_Medica" title="De Materia Medica">De Materia Medica</a></i> describing over 600 herbal cures, forming an influential pharmacopoeia which was used extensively for the following 1,500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early Christians in the Roman Empire incorporated medicine into their theology, ritual practices, and metaphors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="People">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_physicians" title="List of ancient physicians">List of ancient physicians</a></div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 88px"><div style="width: 88px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 86px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates_rubens.jpg" title='Hippocrates (c. 460 - 370 BCE). <div class="gallerytext">

</p>

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 74.666666666667px"><div style="width: 74.666666666667px">
