<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> (129 - 216 CE). Known for their wide insights into anatomy.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul><p><b>Herophilus and Erasistratus</b></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg/220px-Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg/330px-Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg/440px-Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heraklas_Plinthios_Brokhos_Jaw_Sling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>plinthios brochos</i> as described by Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Heraklas" title="Heraklas">Heraklas</a>, a sling for binding a <a href="/wiki/Bone_fracture" title="Bone fracture">fractured</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mandible" title="Human mandible">jaw</a>.  These writings were preserved in one of <a href="/wiki/Oribasius" title="Oribasius">Oribasius</a>' collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cldquipu_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-cldquipu-67">[67]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Two great <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandrians</a> laid the foundations for the scientific study of anatomy and physiology, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herophilus_of_Chalcedon" title="Herophilus of Chalcedon">Herophilus of Chalcedon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erasistratus_of_Ceos" title="Erasistratus of Ceos">Erasistratus of Ceos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Longrigg1993_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Longrigg1993-68">[68]</a></sup> Other Alexandrian surgeons gave us ligature (hemostasis), <a href="/wiki/Lithotomy" title="Lithotomy">lithotomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hernia" title="Hernia">hernia</a> operations, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmic_surgery" title="Ophthalmic surgery">ophthalmic surgery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">plastic surgery</a>, methods of reduction of dislocations and fractures, <a href="/wiki/Tracheotomy" title="Tracheotomy">tracheotomy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mandrake" title="Mandrake">mandrake</a> as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaesthetic" title="Anaesthetic">anaesthetic</a>. Some of what we know of them comes from <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Cornelius_Celsus" title="Aulus Cornelius Celsus">Celsus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> of Pergamum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herophilos" title="Herophilos">Herophilus of Chalcedon</a>, the renowned Alexandrian physician, was one of the pioneers of human anatomy. Though his knowledge of the anatomical structure of the human body was vast, he specialized in the aspects of neural anatomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vallance_1993_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vallance_1993-70">[70]</a></sup> Thus, his experimentation was centered around the anatomical composition of the blood-vascular system and the pulsations that can be analyzed from the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vallance_1993_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vallance_1993-70">[70]</a></sup> Furthermore, the surgical experimentation he administered caused him to become very prominent throughout the field of medicine, as he was one of the first physicians to initiate the exploration and dissection of the human body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Herophilus_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Herophilus-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The banned practice of human dissection was lifted during his time within the scholastic community. This brief moment in the history of Greek medicine allowed him to further study the brain, which he believed was the core of the nervous system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_Herophilus_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica_Herophilus-71">[71]</a></sup> He also distinguished between <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artery" title="Artery">arteries</a>, noting that the latter <a href="/wiki/Pulse" title="Pulse">pulse</a> and the former do not. Thus, while working at the medical school of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, Herophilus placed intelligence in the brain based on his surgical exploration of the body, and he connected the nervous system to motion and sensation. In addition, he and his contemporary, <a href="/wiki/Erasistratus" title="Erasistratus">Erasistratus of Chios</a>, continued to research the role of veins and <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a>. After conducting extensive research, the two Alexandrians mapped out the course of the veins and nerves across the human body. Erasistratus connected the increased complexity of the surface of the human brain compared to other animals to its superior <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">intelligence</a>. He sometimes employed <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experiments</a> to further his research, at one time repeatedly weighing a caged bird, and noting its weight loss between feeding times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason_1962_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mason_1962-72">[72]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Erasistratus" title="Erasistratus">Erasistratus</a>' physiology, air enters the body, is then drawn by the lungs into the heart, where it is transformed into vital spirit, and is then pumped by the arteries throughout the body. Some of this vital spirit reaches the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, where it is transformed into animal spirit, which is then distributed by the nerves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason_1962_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mason_1962-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="5th_to_15th_century_medicine">5th to 15th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 5th to 15th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2819" data-file-width="2396" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg/170px-NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg/255px-NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg/340px-NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NaplesDioscuridesMandrake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mandrake" title="Mandrake">Mandrake</a> (written 'ΜΑΝΔΡΑΓΟΡΑ' in Greek capitals). <i><a href="/wiki/Naples_Dioscurides" title="Naples Dioscurides">Naples Dioscurides</a></i>, 7th century</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Middle East">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_4">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Byzantine medicine</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_medicine" title="Byzantine medicine">Byzantine medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medicine in the medieval Islamic world">Medicine in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<p>Byzantine medicine encompasses the common medical practices of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> from about 400 CE to 1453 CE. Byzantine medicine was notable for building upon the knowledge base developed by its Greco-Roman predecessors. In preserving medical practices from antiquity, Byzantine medicine influenced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">Islamic medicine</a> as well as fostering the Western rebirth of medicine during the Renaissance.
</p><p>Byzantine physicians often compiled and standardized medical knowledge into textbooks. Their records tended to include both diagnostic explanations and technical drawings. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_Compendium_in_Seven_Books" title="Medical Compendium in Seven Books">Medical Compendium in Seven Books</a>, written by the leading physician <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Aegina" title="Paul of Aegina">Paul of Aegina</a>, survived as a particularly thorough source of medical knowledge. This compendium, written in the late seventh century, remained in use as a standard textbook for the following 800 years.
</p><p>Late antiquity ushered in a revolution in medical science, and historical records often mention civilian hospitals (although battlefield medicine and wartime triage were recorded well before Imperial Rome). <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> stood out as a center of medicine during the Middle Ages, which was aided by its crossroads location, wealth, and accumulated knowledge.
</p><p>The first ever known example of separating conjoined twins occurred in the Byzantine Empire in the 10th century. The next example of separating conjoined twins will be first recorded many centuries later in Germany in 1689.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>'s neighbors, the Persian <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sassanid Empire</a>, also made their noteworthy contributions mainly with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Gondishapur" title="Academy of Gondishapur">Academy of Gondeshapur</a>, which was "the most important medical center of the ancient world during the 6th and 7th centuries."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Cyril_Elgood" title="Cyril Elgood">Cyril Elgood</a>, British physician and a historian of medicine in Persia, commented that thanks to medical centers like the Academy of Gondeshapur, "to a very large extent, the credit for the whole hospital system must be given to Persia."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><b>Islamic medicine</b><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medicine in the medieval Islamic world">Medicine in the medieval Islamic world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB,_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="859" data-file-width="796" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png/220px-Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png/330px-Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png/440px-Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB%2C_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ab%C5%AB_Bakr_al-R%C4%81z%C4%AB,_Sayr_mulhimah_min_al-Sharq_wa-al-Gharb.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sketch representing Muslim physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic civilization</a> rose to primacy in medical science as its <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a> contributed significantly to the field of medicine, including <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy" title="Pharmacy">pharmacy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a>. Islamic civilization's contribution to these fields within medicine was a gradual process that took hundreds of years. During the time of the first great Muslim dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> (661-750 CE), these fields that were in their very early stages of development, and not much progress was made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dols_1987_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dols_1987-77">[77]</a></sup> One reason for the limited advancement in medicine during the Umayyad Caliphate was the Caliphate's focus on expansion after the death of Prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> (632 CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamarneh_1962_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamarneh_1962-78">[78]</a></sup> The focus on expansionism redirected resources from other fields, such as medicine. The priority on these factors led a dense amount of the population to believe that God will provide cures for their illnesses and diseases because of the attention on spirituality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamarneh_1962_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamarneh_1962-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>
There were also many other areas of interest during that time before there was a rising interest in the field of medicine. <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan</a>, the fifth caliph of the Umayyad, developed governmental administration, adopted Arabic as the main language, and focused on many other areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, this rising interest in Islamic medicine grew significantly when the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> (750-1258 CE) overthrew the Umayyad Caliphate in 750 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> This change in dynasty from the Umayyad Caliphate to the Abbasid Caliphate served as a turning point towards scientific and medical developments. A big contributor to this is because, under Abbasid rule, there was a great part of the Greek legacy that was transmitted into Arabic which by then, was the main language of Islamic nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamarneh_1962_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hamarneh_1962-78">[78]</a></sup> Because of this, many Islamic physicians were heavily influenced by the works of Greek scholars of Alexandria and Egypt and were able to further expand on those texts to produce new medical pieces of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This period of time is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a> where there was a period of development for development and flourishments of technology, commerce, and sciences including medicine. Additionally, during this time the creation of the first Islamic Hospital in 805 CE by the Abbasid caliph <a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a> in Baghdad was recounted as a glorious event of the Golden Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dols_1987_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dols_1987-77">[77]</a></sup> This hospital in Baghdad contributed immensely to Baghdad's success and also provided educational opportunities for Islamic physicians. During the Islamic Golden Age, there were many famous Islamic physicians that paved the way for medical advancements and understandings. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi</a> (965-1040 CE), sometimes referred to as the father of modern optics, is the author of the monumental <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Optics" title="Book of Optics">Book of Optics</a> and also was known for his work in differentiating smallpox from measles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> However, this would not be possible without the influence from many different areas of the world that influenced the Arabs.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/170px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/255px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cheshm_manuscript.jpg/340px-Cheshm_manuscript.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheshm_manuscript.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> manuscript, <i>Anatomy of the Eye</i>, by al-Mutadibih, 1200 CE</div></div></div>
<p>The Arabs were influenced by ancient Indian, Persian, Greek, Roman and Byzantine medical practices, and helped them develop further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> were pre-eminent authorities. The translation of 129 of Galen's works into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> by the Nestorian Christian <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a> and his assistants, and in particular Galen's insistence on a rational systematic approach to medicine, set the template for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">Islamic medicine</a>, which rapidly spread throughout the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Arab Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarton_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarton-84">[84]</a></sup> Its most famous physicians included the Persian polymaths <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Muhammad ibn Zakarīya al-Rāzi</a>  and  <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>,  who wrote more than 40 works on health, medicine, and well-being. Taking leads from Greece and Rome, Islamic scholars kept both the art and science of medicine alive and moving forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kill_or_cure_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-kill_or_cure-85">[85]</a></sup> Persian polymath <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> has also been called the "father of medicine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> He wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> which became a standard medical text at many medieval European <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">universities</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> considered one of the most famous books in the history of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canon_of_Medicine" title="The Canon of Medicine">The Canon of Medicine</a></i> presents an overview of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Medicine in the medieval Islamic world">medical knowledge of the medieval Islamic world</a>, which had been influenced by earlier traditions including <a href="/wiki/Medicine_in_ancient_Rome" title="Medicine in ancient Rome">Greco-Roman medicine</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IranicaX_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-IranicaX-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Iranian_medicine" title="Ancient Iranian medicine">Persian medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Chinese medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Indian medicine</a>. Persian physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">al-Rāzi</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tschanz_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tschanz-90">[90]</a></sup> was one of the first to question the Greek theory of <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humorism</a>, which nevertheless remained influential in both medieval Western and medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_medicine" title="Islamic medicine">Islamic medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Some volumes of al-Rāzi's work <i>Al-Mansuri</i>, namely "On Surgery" and "A General Book on Therapy", became part of the medical curriculum in European universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Additionally, he has been described as a doctor's doctor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> the father of <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elgood_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elgood-95">[95]</a></sup> and a pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a>. For example, he was the first to recognize the reaction of the eye's pupil to light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elgood_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Elgood-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to contributions to humanity's understanding of human anatomy, Islamicate scientists and scholars, physicians specifically, played an invaluable role in the development of the modern hospital system, creating the foundations on which more contemporary medical professionals would build models of public health systems in Europe and elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majeed_2005_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Majeed_2005-96">[96]</a></sup> During the time of the Safavid empire (16th–18th centuries) in Iran and the Mughal empire (16th–19th centuries) in India, Muslim scholars radically transformed the institution of the hospital, creating an environment in which rapidly developing medical knowledge of the time could be passed among students and teachers from a wide range of cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speziale,_F._2012_pp._2-4_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Speziale,_F._2012_pp._2-4-97">[97]</a></sup> There were two main schools of thought with patient care at the time. These included humoral physiology from the Persians and Ayurvedic practice. After these theories were translated from Sanskrit to Persian and vice-versa, hospitals could have a mix of culture and techniques. This allowed for a sense of collaborative medicine. Hospitals became increasingly common during this period as wealthy patrons commonly founded them. Many features that are still in use today, such as an emphasis on hygiene, a staff fully dedicated to the care of patients, and separation of individual patients from each other were developed in Islamicate hospitals long before they came into practice in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> At the time, the patient care aspects of hospitals in Europe had not taken effect. European hospitals were places of religion rather than institutions of science. As was the case with much of the scientific work done by Islamicate scholars, many of these novel developments in medical practice were transmitted to European cultures hundreds of years after they had long been utilized throughout the Islamicate world. Although Islamicate scientists were responsible for discovering much of the knowledge that allows the hospital system to function safely today, European scholars who built on this work still receive the majority of the credit historically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majeed_2005_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Majeed_2005-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the development of scientific medical practices in the Islamicate empires, medical care was mainly performed by religious figures such as priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majeed_2005_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Majeed_2005-96">[96]</a></sup> Without a profound understanding of how infectious diseases worked and why sickness spread from person to person, these early attempts at caring for the ill and injured often did more harm than good. Contrarily, with the development of new and safer practices by Islamicate scholars and physicians in Arabian hospitals, ideas vital for the effective care of patients were developed, learned, and transmitted widely. Hospitals served as a way to spread these novel and necessary practices, some of which included separation of men and women patients, use of pharmacies for storing and keeping track of medications, keeping of patient records, and personal and institutional sanitation and hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majeed_2005_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-Majeed_2005-96">[96]</a></sup> Much of this knowledge was recorded and passed on through Islamicate medical texts, many of which were carried to Europe and translated for the use of European medical workers. The Tasrif, written by surgeon Abu Al-Qasim Al-Zahrawi, was translated into Latin; it became one of the most important medical texts in European universities during the Middle Ages and contained useful information on surgical techniques and spread of bacterial infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majeed_2005_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-Majeed_2005-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hospital was a typical institution included in the majority of Muslim cities, and although they were often physically attached to religious institutions, they were not themselves places of religious practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Speziale,_F._2012_pp._2-4_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Speziale,_F._2012_pp._2-4-97">[97]</a></sup> Rather, they served as facilities in which education and scientific innovation could flourish. If they had places of worship, they were secondary to the medical side of the hospital. Islamicate hospitals, along with observatories used for astronomical science, were some of the most important points of exchange for the spread of scientific knowledge. Undoubtedly, the hospital system developed in the Islamicate world played an invaluable role in the creation and evolution of the hospitals we as a society know and depend on today.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_2">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine_of_Western_Europe" title="Medieval medicine of Western Europe">Medieval medicine of Western Europe</a></div>
<p>After 400 CE, the study and practice of medicine in the Western Roman Empire went into deep decline.  Medical services were provided, especially for the poor, in the thousands of monastic hospitals that sprang up across Europe, but the care was rudimentary and mainly palliative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>  Most of the writings of Galen and Hippocrates were lost to the West, with the summaries and compendia of St. Isidore of Seville being the primary channel for transmitting Greek medical ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>  The Carolingian renaissance brought increased contact with Byzantium and a greater awareness of ancient medicine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> but only with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelfth-century_renaissance" title="Twelfth-century renaissance">twelfth-century renaissance</a> and the new translations coming from Muslim and Jewish sources in Spain, and the fifteenth-century flood of resources after the fall of Constantinople did the West fully recover its acquaintance with classical antiquity.
</p><p>Greek and Roman taboos had meant that dissection was usually banned in ancient times, but in the Middle Ages it changed: medical teachers and students at Bologna began to open human bodies, and <a href="/wiki/Mondino_de_Luzzi" title="Mondino de Luzzi">Mondino de Luzzi</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1275</span>–1326) produced the first known anatomy textbook based on human dissection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Numbers_2009_45_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Numbers_2009_45-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.harvard.edu_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-news.harvard.edu-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Wallis identifies a prestige hierarchy with university educated physicians on top, followed by learned surgeons; craft-trained surgeons; barber surgeons; itinerant specialists such as dentist and oculists; empirics; and midwives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions">Institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="1593" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg/220px-ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg/330px-ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg/440px-ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ScuolaMedicaMiniatura.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A miniature depicting the <a href="/wiki/Schola_Medica_Salernitana" title="Schola Medica Salernitana">Schola Medica Salernitana</a> in <a href="/wiki/Salerno" title="Salerno">Salerno</a> in Italy</div></div></div>
<p>The first medical schools were opened in the 9th century, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Schola_Medica_Salernitana" title="Schola Medica Salernitana">Schola Medica Salernitana</a> at Salerno in southern Italy. The cosmopolitan influences from Greek, Latin, Arabic, and Hebrew sources gave it an international reputation as the Hippocratic City.  Students from wealthy families came for three years of preliminary studies and five of medical studies. The medicine, following the laws of Federico II, that he founded in 1224  the university and improved the Schola Salernitana, in the period between 1200 and 1400, it had in Sicily (so-called Sicilian Middle Ages) a particular development so much to create a true school of Jewish medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of which, after a legal examination, was conferred to a Jewish Sicilian woman, <a href="/wiki/Virdimura" title="Virdimura">Virdimura</a>, wife of another physician Pasquale of Catania, the historical record of before woman officially trained to exercise of the medical profession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> the training of physicians began in 1219. The Italian city attracted students from across Europe.  Taddeo Alderotti built a tradition of medical education that established the characteristic features of Italian learned medicine and was copied by medical schools elsewhere.<a href="/wiki/Turisanus" title="Turisanus">Turisanus</a> (d. 1320) was his student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a> was founded about 1220 by walkouts from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a>, and began teaching medicine in 1222. It played a leading role in the identification and treatment of diseases and ailments, specializing in autopsies and the inner workings of the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Starting in 1595, Padua's famous anatomical theatre drew artists and scientists studying the human body during public dissections. The intensive study of Galen led to critiques of Galen modeled on his own writing, as in the first book of Vesalius's <i>De humani corporis fabrica.</i> <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> held the chair of Surgery and Anatomy (<i>explicator chirurgiae</i>) and in 1543 published his anatomical discoveries in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Humani_Corporis_Fabrica" title="De Humani Corporis Fabrica">De Humani Corporis Fabrica</a></i>. He portrayed the human body as an interdependent system of organ groupings.  The book triggered great public interest in dissections and caused many other European cities to establish anatomical theatres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2548" data-file-width="4530" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg/220px-Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg/330px-Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg/440px-Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collegium_Maius_2017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The oldest Polish Collegium Medicum at <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a> founded in 1364</div></div></div>
<p>By the thirteenth century, the medical school at Montpellier began to eclipse the Salernitan school. In the 12th century, universities were founded in Italy, France, and England, which soon developed schools of medicine. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Montpellier" title="University of Montpellier">University of Montpellier</a> in France and Italy's <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> were leading schools. Nearly all the learning was from lectures and readings in Hippocrates, Galen, Avicenna, and Aristotle. In later centuries, the importance of universities founded in the late Middle Ages gradually increased, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Charles_University" title="Charles University">Charles University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> (established in 1348), <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cracow" title="Cracow">Cracow</a> (1364), <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a> (1365), <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_University" title="Heidelberg University">Heidelberg University</a> (1386) and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Greifswald" title="University of Greifswald">University of Greifswald</a> (1456).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="People_2">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1376, in Sicily, it was historically given, in relationship to the laws of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Federico II</a> that they foresaw an examination with a regal errand of physicists, the first qualification to the exercise of the medicine to a woman, <a href="/wiki/Virdimura" title="Virdimura">Virdimura</a> a Jewish woman of Catania, whose document is preserved in <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a> to the Italian national archives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="16th_century_medicine">16th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 16th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_5">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>England</b>
</p><p>In England, there were but three small hospitals after 1550. Pelling and Webster estimate that in London in the 1580 to 1600 period, out of a population of nearly 200,000 people, there were about 500 medical practitioners.  Nurses and midwives are not included. There were about 50 physicians, 100 licensed surgeons, 100 apothecaries, and 250 additional unlicensed practitioners. In the last category about 25% were women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>  All across England—and indeed all of the world—the vast majority of the people in city, town or countryside depended for medical care on local amateurs with no professional training but with a reputation as wise healers who could diagnose problems and advise sick people what to do—and perhaps set broken bones, pull a tooth, give some traditional herbs or brews or perform a little magic to cure what ailed them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="People_3">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_century_medicine">17th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: 17th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_3">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
The Renaissance brought an intense focus on scholarship to Christian Europe. A major effort to translate the Arabic and Greek scientific works into Latin emerged. Europeans gradually became experts not only in the ancient writings of the Romans and Greeks, but in the contemporary writings of Islamic scientists. During the later centuries of the Renaissance came an increase in experimental investigation, particularly in the field of dissection and body examination, thus advancing our knowledge of human anatomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="901" data-file-width="1293" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/220px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/330px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/440px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An experiment from <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">de Motu Cordis</a></i>, 1628</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1676" data-file-width="1007" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png/170px-Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png/255px-Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png/340px-Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leeuwenhoek_Microscope.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a> of the 1670s</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideas_2">Ideas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ideas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><b>Animalcules:</b> In 1677 <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> identified "animalcules", which we now know as microorganisms, within their paper "letter on the protozoa".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Blood circulation:</b> In 1628 the English physician <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a> made a ground-breaking discovery when he correctly described the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulation of the blood</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Exercitatio_Anatomica_de_Motu_Cordis_et_Sanguinis_in_Animalibus" title="Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus">Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus</a></i>. Before this time the most useful manual in medicine used both by students and expert physicians was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscorides" title="Dioscorides">Dioscorides</a>' <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Materia_Medica" title="De Materia Medica">De Materia Medica</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Pharmacopoeia" title="Pharmacopoeia">pharmacopoeia</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inventions">Inventions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Inventions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><b>Microscopes:</b> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a> were first observed with a <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a> by <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> in 1676, initiating the scientific field of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brock-112">[112]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_2">Institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> the curriculum was revised and strengthened in 1560–1590.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>  A representative professor was <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_Aranzi" title="Julius Caesar Aranzi">Julius Caesar Aranzi</a> (Arantius) (1530–1589). He became Professor of Anatomy and Surgery at the University of Bologna in 1556, where he established anatomy as a major branch of medicine for the first time. Aranzi combined anatomy with a description of pathological processes, based largely on his own research, Galen, and the work of his contemporary Italians.  Aranzi discovered the 'Nodules of Aranzio' in the semilunar valves of the heart and wrote the first description of the superior levator palpebral and the coracobrachialis muscles. His books (in Latin) covered surgical techniques for many conditions, including <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nasal_polyp" title="Nasal polyp">nasal polyp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goitre" title="Goitre">goitre</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumours</a> to <a href="/wiki/Phimosis" title="Phimosis">phimosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ascites" title="Ascites">ascites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemorrhoid" title="Hemorrhoid">haemorrhoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anorectal_abscess" title="Anorectal abscess">anal abscess</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fistula" title="Fistula">fistulae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="People_4">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Women</b>
</p><p>Catholic women played large roles in health and healing in medieval and early modern Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>  A life as a nun was a prestigious role; wealthy families provided dowries for their daughters, and these funded the convents, while the nuns provided free nursing care for the poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11618104_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11618104-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Catholic elites provided hospital services because of their theology of salvation that good works were the route to heaven. The Protestant reformers rejected the notion that rich men could gain God's grace through good works—and thereby escape purgatory—by providing cash endowments to charitable institutions. They also rejected the Catholic idea that the poor patients earned grace and salvation through their suffering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>  Protestants generally closed all the convents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> and most of the hospitals, sending women home to become housewives, often against their will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>  On the other hand, local officials recognized the public value of hospitals, and some were continued in Protestant lands, but without monks or nuns and in the control of local governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>In London, the crown allowed two hospitals to continue their charitable work, under nonreligious control of city officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>  The convents were all shut down but Harkness finds that women—some of them former nuns—were part of a new system that delivered essential medical services to people outside their family. They were employed by parishes and hospitals, as well as by private families, and provided nursing care as well as some medical, pharmaceutical, and surgical services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, in Catholic lands such as France, rich families continued to fund convents and monasteries, and enrolled their daughters as nuns who provided free health services to the poor.  Nursing was a religious role for the nurse, and there was little call for science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21724643_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21724643-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century_medicine">18th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: 18th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_4">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Events">Events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>European Age of Englightenment</b>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, the 18th century, science was held in high esteem and physicians upgraded their social status by becoming more scientific.  The health field was crowded with self-trained barber-surgeons, apothecaries, midwives, drug peddlers, and charlatans.
</p><p>Across Europe medical schools relied primarily on lectures and readings.  The final year student would have limited clinical experience by trailing the professor through the wards.  Laboratory work was uncommon, and dissections were rarely done because of legal restrictions on cadavers.  Most schools were small, and only Edinburgh, Scotland, with 11,000 alumni, produced large numbers of graduates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_6">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Spain and the Spanish Empire</b></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="494" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg/220px-FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg/330px-FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg/440px-FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FlorentineCodex_BK12_F54_smallpox.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> in Franciscan <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_de_Sahag%C3%BAn" title="Bernardino de Sahagún">Bernardino de Sahagún</a>'s history of the conquest of Mexico, Book XII of the <i><a href="/wiki/Florentine_Codex" title="Florentine Codex">Florentine Codex</a></i>, from the defeated Aztecs' point of view</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a>, the viceregal capital of Mexico City was a site of medical training for physicians and the creation of hospitals. Epidemic disease had decimated indigenous populations starting with the early sixteenth-century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec empire">Spanish conquest of the Aztec empire</a>, when a black auxiliary in the armed forces of conqueror <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a>, with an active case of <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, set off a virgin land epidemic among indigenous peoples, Spanish allies and enemies alike. Aztec emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuitlahuac" title="Cuitlahuac">Cuitlahuac</a> died of smallpox.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Disease was a significant factor in the Spanish conquest elsewhere as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6204" data-file-width="4124" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/170px-Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/255px-Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg/340px-Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dibujo_Escudo_de_Armas_de_M%C3%A9xico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mexico City epidemic of 1737, with elites calling on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_of_Guadalupe" title="Virgin of Guadalupe">Virgin of Guadalupe</a></div></div></div>
<p>Medical education instituted at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_and_Pontifical_University_of_Mexico" title="Royal and Pontifical University of Mexico">Royal and Pontifical University of Mexico</a> chiefly served the needs of urban elites. Male and female <i>curanderos</i> or lay practitioners, attended to the ills of the popular classes. The Spanish crown began regulating the medical profession just a few years after the conquest, setting up the Royal Tribunal of the Protomedicato, a board for licensing medical personnel in 1527.  Licensing became more systematic after 1646 with physicians, druggists, surgeons, and bleeders requiring a license before they could publicly practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Crown regulation of medical practice became more general in the Spanish empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elites and the popular classes alike called on divine intervention in personal and society-wide health crises, such as the epidemic of 1737. The intervention of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgin_of_Guadalupe" title="Virgin of Guadalupe">Virgin of Guadalupe</a> was depicted in a scene of dead and dying Indians, with elites on their knees praying for her aid. In the late eighteenth century, the crown began implementing secularizing policies on the Iberian peninsula and its overseas empire to control disease more systematically and scientifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Spanish Quest for Medicinal Spices</b>
</p><p>Botanical medicines also became popular during the 16th, 17th, and 18th Centuries. Spanish pharmaceutical books during this time contain medicinal recipes consisting of spices, herbs, and other botanical products. For example, nutmeg oil was documented for curing stomach ailments and cardamom oil was believed to relieve intestinal ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2006_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2006-134">[134]</a></sup> During the rise of the global trade market, spices and herbs, along with many other goods, that were indigenous to different territories began to appear in different locations across the globe. Herbs and spices were especially popular for their utility in cooking and medicines. As a result of this popularity and increased demand for spices, some areas in Asia, like China and Indonesia, became hubs for spice cultivation and trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_1998_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_1998-135">[135]</a></sup> The Spanish Empire also wanted to benefit from the international spice trade, so they looked towards their American colonies.
</p><p>The Spanish American colonies became an area where the Spanish searched to discover new spices and indigenous American medicinal recipes. The <a href="/wiki/Florentine_Codex" title="Florentine Codex">Florentine Codex</a>, a 16th-century ethnographic research study in Mesoamerica by the Spanish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan_friar" title="Franciscan friar">Franciscan friar</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_de_Sahag%C3%BAn" title="Bernardino de Sahagún">Bernardino de Sahagún</a>, is a major contribution to the history of <a href="/wiki/Nahuas" title="Nahuas">Nahua</a> medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The Spanish did discover many spices and herbs new to them, some of which were reportedly similar to Asian spices.  A Spanish physician by the name of <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Monardes" title="Nicolás Monardes">Nicolás Monardes</a> studied many of the American spices coming into Spain. He documented many of the new American spices and their medicinal properties in his survey <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_medicinal_de_las_cosas_que_se_traen_de_nuestras_Indias_Occidentales" title="Historia medicinal de las cosas que se traen de nuestras Indias Occidentales">Historia medicinal de las cosas que se traen de nuestras Indias Occidentales</a></i>. For example, Monardes describes the "Long Pepper" (Pimienta luenga), found along the coasts of the countries that are now known Panama and Colombia, as a pepper that was more flavorful, healthy, and spicy in comparison to the Eastern black pepper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2006_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2006-134">[134]</a></sup> The Spanish interest in American spices can first be seen in the commissioning of the <i><a href="/wiki/Libellus_de_Medicinalibus_Indorum_Herbis" title="Libellus de Medicinalibus Indorum Herbis">Libellus de Medicinalibus Indorum Herbis</a></i>, which was a Spanish-American codex describing indigenous American spices and herbs and describing the ways that these were used in natural Aztec medicines. The codex was commissioned in the year 1552 by Francisco de Mendoza, the son of <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Mendoza" title="Antonio de Mendoza">Antonio de Mendoza</a>, who was the first Viceroy of New Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2006_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2006-134">[134]</a></sup> Francisco de Mendoza was interested in studying the properties of these herbs and spices, so that he would be able to profit from the trade of these herbs and the medicines that could be produced by them.
</p><p>Francisco de Mendoza recruited the help of Monardez in studying the traditional medicines of the indigenous people living in what was then the Spanish colonies. Monardez researched these medicines and performed experiments to discover the possibilities of spice cultivation and medicine creation in the Spanish colonies. The Spanish transplanted some herbs from Asia, but only a few foreign crops were successfully grown in the Spanish Colonies. One notable crop brought from Asia and successfully grown in the Spanish colonies was ginger, as it was considered Hispaniola's number 1 crop at the end of the 16th Century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Vos_2006_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-De_Vos_2006-134">[134]</a></sup> The Spanish Empire did profit from cultivating herbs and spices, but they also introduced pre-Columbian American medicinal knowledge to Europe. Other Europeans were inspired by the actions of Spain and decided to try to establish a botanical transplant system in colonies that they controlled, however, these subsequent attempts were not successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_1998_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frank_1998-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><b>United Kingdom</b> <b>and the British Empire</b></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2782" data-file-width="1680" decoding="async" height="364" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg/220px-18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg/330px-18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg/440px-18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:18th_Century_Recipes_f.459_-_Medical_remedies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>18th-century medical remedies collected by a British Gentry family</div></div></div>
<p>The London Dispensary opened in 1696, the first clinic in the British Empire to dispense medicines to poor sick people. The innovation was slow to catch on, but new dispensaries were open in the 1770s.  In the colonies, small hospitals opened in Philadelphia in 1752, New York in 1771, and Boston (<a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_General_Hospital" title="Massachusetts General Hospital">Massachusetts General Hospital</a>) in 1811.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1852" data-file-width="2660" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg/220px-Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg/330px-Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg/440px-Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guy%27s_Hospital00.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Guy%27s_Hospital" title="Guy's Hospital">Guy's Hospital</a> in 1820</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Guy%27s_Hospital" title="Guy's Hospital">Guy's Hospital</a>, the first great British hospital with a modern foundation opened in 1721 in London, with funding from businessman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Guy" title="Thomas Guy">Thomas Guy</a>. It had been preceded by <a href="/wiki/St_Bartholomew%27s_Hospital" title="St Bartholomew's Hospital">St Bartholomew's Hospital</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St_Thomas%27s_Hospital" title="St Thomas's Hospital">St Thomas's Hospital</a>, both medieval foundations. In 1821 a bequest of £200,000 by William Hunt in 1829 funded expansion for an additional hundred beds at Guy's. <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sharp_(surgeon)" title="Samuel Sharp (surgeon)">Samuel Sharp</a> (1709–78), a surgeon at Guy's Hospital from 1733 to 1757, was internationally famous; his <i>A Treatise on the Operations of Surgery</i> (1st ed., 1739), was the first British study focused exclusively on operative technique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>English physician <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Percival" title="Thomas Percival">Thomas Percival</a> (1740–1804) wrote a comprehensive system of medical conduct, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_Ethics;_or,_a_Code_of_Institutes_and_Precepts,_Adapted_to_the_Professional_Conduct_of_Physicians_and_Surgeons" title="Medical Ethics; or, a Code of Institutes and Precepts, Adapted to the Professional Conduct of Physicians and Surgeons">Medical Ethics; or, a Code of Institutes and Precepts, Adapted to the Professional Conduct of Physicians and Surgeons</a></i> (1803) that set the standard for many textbooks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century_medicine">19th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 19th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germ_theory_and_bacteriology">Germ theory and bacteriology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Germ theory and bacteriology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1830s in Italy,  <a href="/wiki/Agostino_Bassi" title="Agostino Bassi">Agostino Bassi</a> traced the silkworm disease <a href="/wiki/Muscardine" title="Muscardine">muscardine</a> to microorganisms.  Meanwhile, in Germany, <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a> led research on <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcoholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>, proposing that living microorganisms were responsible.
Leading chemists, such as <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>, seeking solely <a href="/wiki/Physical_chemistry" title="Physical chemistry">physicochemical</a> explanations, derided this claim and alleged that Schwann was regressing to <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>.
</p><p>In 1847 in Vienna, <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> (1818–1865), dramatically reduced the death rate of new mothers (due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childbed_fever" title="Childbed fever">childbed fever</a>) by requiring physicians to <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">clean their hands</a> before attending <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>, yet his principles were marginalized and attacked by professional peers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> At that time most people still believed that infections were caused by foul odors called <a href="/wiki/Miasma_theory" title="Miasma theory">miasmas</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1547" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/220px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/330px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/440px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> experimenting on <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, c. 1870</div></div></div>
<p>French scientist <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> confirmed <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Schwann</a>'s fermentation experiments in 1857 and afterwards supported the hypothesis that yeast were microorganisms. Moreover, he suggested that such a process might also explain contagious disease. In 1860, Pasteur's report on <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> fermentation of <a href="/wiki/Butyric_acid" title="Butyric acid">butyric acid</a> motivated fellow Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Casimir_Davaine" title="Casimir Davaine">Casimir Davaine</a> to identify a similar species (which he called <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">bacteridia</a></i></span>) as the pathogen of the deadly disease <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>. Others dismissed "<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bacteridia</i></span>" as a mere byproduct of the disease. British surgeon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a>, however, took these findings seriously and subsequently introduced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisepsis" title="Antisepsis">antisepsis</a> to wound treatment in 1865.
</p><p>German physician <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>, noting fellow German <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Cohn" title="Ferdinand Cohn">Ferdinand Cohn</a>'s report of a spore stage of a certain bacterial species, traced the life cycle of <a href="/wiki/Casimir_Davaine" title="Casimir Davaine">Davaine</a>'s <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bacteridia</i></span>, identified spores, inoculated laboratory animals with them, and reproduced anthrax—a breakthrough for <a href="/wiki/Experimental_pathology" title="Experimental pathology">experimental pathology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>.  Pasteur's group added <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">ecological</a> investigations confirming spores' role in the natural setting, while Koch published a landmark treatise in 1878 on the bacterial pathology of wounds.  In 1881, Koch reported discovery of the "<a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">tubercle bacillus</a>", cementing germ theory and Koch's acclaim.
</p><p>Upon the outbreak of a <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemic in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Egypt" title="Alexandria, Egypt">Alexandria, Egypt</a>, two medical missions went to investigate and attend the sick, one was sent out by Pasteur and the other led by Koch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guenel_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guenel-141">[141]</a></sup>  Koch's group returned in 1883, having successfully discovered the <a href="/wiki/Vibrio_cholerae" title="Vibrio cholerae">cholera pathogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guenel_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guenel-141">[141]</a></sup>  In Germany, however, Koch's bacteriologists had to vie against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Max_von_Pettenkofer" title="Max von Pettenkofer">Max von Pettenkofer</a>, Germany's leading proponent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miasmatic_theory" title="Miasmatic theory">miasmatic theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser-142">[142]</a></sup>  Pettenkofer conceded bacteria's casual involvement, but maintained that other, environmental factors were required to turn it pathogenic, and opposed water treatment as a misdirected effort amid more important ways to improve public health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser-142">[142]</a></sup>  The massive cholera epidemic in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> in 1892 devastated Pettenkoffer's position, and yielded German <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> to "Koch's bacteriology".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morabia-Oppenheimer-Susser-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>On losing the 1883 rivalry in Alexandria, Pasteur switched research direction, and introduced his third vaccine—<a href="/wiki/Rabies_vaccine" title="Rabies vaccine">rabies vaccine</a>—the first vaccine for humans since <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Jenner</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">for smallpox</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guenel_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guenel-141">[141]</a></sup>  From across the globe, donations poured in, funding the founding of <a href="/wiki/Pasteur_Institute" title="Pasteur Institute">Pasteur Institute</a>, the globe's first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomedical" title="Biomedical">biomedical</a> institute, which opened in 1888.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guenel_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guenel-141">[141]</a></sup>  Along with Koch's bacteriologists, Pasteur's group—which preferred the term <i>microbiology</i>—led medicine into the new era of "scientific medicine" upon bacteriology and germ theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guenel_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-Guenel-141">[141]</a></sup>  Accepted from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jakob_Henle" title="Jakob Henle">Jakob Henle</a>, Koch's steps to confirm a species' pathogenicity became famed as "<a href="/wiki/Koch%27s_postulates" title="Koch's postulates">Koch's postulates</a>".  Although his proposed tuberculosis treatment, <a href="/wiki/Tuberculin" title="Tuberculin">tuberculin</a>, seemingly failed, it soon was used to test for infection with the <a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">involved species</a>.  In 1905, Koch was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a>, and remains renowned as the founder of <a href="/wiki/Medical_microbiology" title="Medical microbiology">medical microbiology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nursing">Nursing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Nursing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The breakthrough to professionalization based on knowledge of advanced medicine was led by <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a> in England. She resolved to provide more advanced training than she saw on the Continent. At Kaiserswerth, where the first German nursing schools were founded in 1836 by <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fliedner" title="Theodor Fliedner">Theodor Fliedner</a>, she said, "The nursing was nil and the hygiene horrible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>)  Britain's male doctors preferred the old system, but Nightingale won out and her Nightingale Training School opened in 1860 and became a model. The Nightingale solution depended on the patronage of upper-class women, and they proved eager to serve. Royalty became involved. In 1902 the wife of the British king took control of the nursing unit of the British army, became its president, and renamed it after herself as the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Alexandra%27s_Royal_Army_Nursing_Corps" title="Queen Alexandra's Royal Army Nursing Corps">Queen Alexandra's Royal Army Nursing Corps</a>; when she died the next queen became president. Today its Colonel In Chief is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sophie,_Countess_of_Wessex" title="Sophie, Countess of Wessex">Sophie, Countess of Wessex</a>, the daughter-in-law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Queen Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a>.
