In the United States, upper-middle-class women who already supported hospitals promoted nursing. The new profession proved highly attractive to women of all backgrounds, and schools of nursing opened in the late 19th century. They were soon a function of large hospitals<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, where they provided a steady stream of low-paid idealistic workers. The International Red Cross began operations in numerous countries in the late 19th century, promoting nursing as an ideal profession for middle-class women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical_methods">Statistical methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Statistical methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale-mortality.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3826" data-file-width="6996" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/220px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/330px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nightingale-mortality.jpg/440px-Nightingale-mortality.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nightingale-mortality.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_area_diagram" title="Polar area diagram">Diagram</a> of the causes of mortality in the army in the East</i>" by Florence Nightingale.</div></div></div>
<p>A major breakthrough in epidemiology came with the introduction of statistical maps and graphs. They allowed careful analysis of seasonality issues in disease incidents, and the maps allowed public health officials to identify critical loci for the dissemination of disease.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Snow_(physician)" title="John Snow (physician)">John Snow</a> in London developed the methods. In 1849, he observed that the symptoms of cholera, which had already claimed around 500 lives within a month, were vomiting and diarrhoea. He concluded that the source of contamination must be through ingestion, rather than inhalation as was previously thought. It was this insight that resulted in the removal of The Pump On Broad Street, after which deaths from cholera plummeted afterwards. English nurse <a href="/wiki/Florence_Nightingale" title="Florence Nightingale">Florence Nightingale</a> pioneered analysis of large amounts of statistical data, using graphs and tables, regarding the condition of thousands of patients in the Crimean War to evaluate the efficacy of hospital services. Her methods proved convincing and led to reforms in military and civilian hospitals, usually with the full support of the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>&gt;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 19th and early 20th century English statisticians led by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Galton" title="Francis Galton">Francis Galton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Pearson" title="Karl Pearson">Karl Pearson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Fisher" title="Ronald Fisher">Ronald Fisher</a> developed the mathematical tools such as correlations and hypothesis tests that made possible much more sophisticated analysis of statistical data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the U.S. Civil War the Sanitary Commission collected enormous amounts of statistical data, and opened up the problems of storing information for fast access and mechanically searching for data patterns.  The pioneer was <a href="/wiki/John_Shaw_Billings" title="John Shaw Billings">John Shaw Billings</a> (1838–1913). A senior surgeon in the war, Billings built the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_the_Surgeon_General%27s_Office" title="Library of the Surgeon General's Office">Library of the Surgeon General's Office</a> (now the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Library_of_Medicine" title="National Library of Medicine">National Library of Medicine</a>), the centerpiece of modern medical information systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Billings figured out how to mechanically analyze medical and demographic data by turning facts into numbers and punching the numbers onto cardboard cards that could be sorted and counted by machine. The applications were developed by his assistant <a href="/wiki/Herman_Hollerith" title="Herman Hollerith">Herman Hollerith</a>; Hollerith invented the punch card and counter-sorter system that dominated statistical data manipulation until the 1970s.  Hollerith's company became <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">International Business Machines (IBM)</a> in 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychiatry">Psychiatry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Psychiatry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_psychiatry" title="History of psychiatry">History of psychiatry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg/220px-RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg/330px-RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg/440px-RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RetreatOriginalBuildingssm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Quaker-run <a href="/wiki/The_Retreat" title="The Retreat">York Retreat</a>, founded in 1796, gained international prominence as a centre for moral treatment and a model of asylum reform following the publication of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Tuke_(reformer)" title="Samuel Tuke (reformer)">Samuel Tuke</a>'s <i>Description of the Retreat</i> (1813).</div></div></div>
<p>Until the nineteenth century, the care of the <a href="/wiki/Insanity" title="Insanity">insane</a> was largely a communal and family responsibility rather than a medical one. The vast majority of the <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mentally ill</a> were treated in domestic contexts with only the most unmanageable or burdensome likely to be institutionally confined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> This situation was transformed radically from the late eighteenth century as, amid changing cultural conceptions of madness, a new-found optimism in the curability of insanity within the asylum setting emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> Increasingly, lunacy was perceived less as a <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiological</a> condition than as a mental and moral one<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> to which the correct response was persuasion, aimed at inculcating internal restraint, rather than external coercion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> This new therapeutic sensibility, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Moral_treatment" title="Moral treatment">moral treatment</a>, was epitomised in French physician <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Pinel" title="Philippe Pinel">Philippe Pinel</a>'s quasi-mythological unchaining of the lunatics of the <a href="/wiki/Bic%C3%AAtre_Hospital" title="Bicêtre Hospital">Bicêtre Hospital</a> in Paris<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> and realised in an institutional setting with the foundation in 1796 of the Quaker-run York <a href="/wiki/The_Retreat" title="The Retreat">Retreat</a> in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bynum_2006_p198–199_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bynum_2006_p198–199-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond,_c1850-58.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2633" data-file-width="1942" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg/170px-Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg/255px-Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg/340px-Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond%2C_c1850-58.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asylum_patient_by_Hugh_Welch_Diamond,_c1850-58.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Patient, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springfield_Hospital" title="Springfield Hospital">Surrey County Lunatic Asylum</a>, c. 1850–58. The asylum population in England and Wales rose from 1,027 in 1827 to 74,004 in 1900.</div></div></div>
<p>From the early nineteenth century, as lay-led lunacy reform movements gained in influence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> ever more state governments in the West extended their authority and responsibility over the mentally ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-otto_marx_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-otto_marx-158">[158]</a></sup> Small-scale asylums, conceived as instruments to reshape both the mind and behaviour of the disturbed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayward2011_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward2011-159">[159]</a></sup> proliferated across these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews2004_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews2004-160">[160]</a></sup> By the 1830s, moral treatment, together with the asylum itself, became increasingly medicalised<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> and asylum doctors began to establish a distinct medical identity with the establishment in the 1840s of associations for their members in France, Germany, the United Kingdom and America, together with the founding of medico-psychological journals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bynum_2006_p198–199_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bynum_2006_p198–199-22">[22]</a></sup> Medical optimism in the capacity of the asylum to cure insanity soured by the close of the nineteenth century as the growth of the asylum population far outstripped that of the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-asylum_numbers_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-asylum_numbers-162">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup> Processes of long-term institutional segregation, allowing for the psychiatric conceptualisation of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_history_of_disease" title="Natural history of disease">natural course</a> of mental illness, supported the perspective that the insane were a distinct population, subject to mental pathologies stemming from specific medical causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayward2011_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hayward2011-159">[159]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Social_degeneration" title="Social degeneration">degeneration</a> theory grew in influence from the mid-nineteenth century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup> heredity was seen as the central causal element in chronic mental illness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup> and, with national asylum systems overcrowded and insanity apparently undergoing an inexorable rise, the focus of psychiatric therapeutics shifted from a concern with treating the individual to maintaining the racial and biological health of national populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Emil_Kraepelin" title="Emil Kraepelin">Emil Kraepelin</a> (1856–1926) introduced new medical categories of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mental_illness" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>, which eventually came into <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatric</a> usage despite their basis in behavior rather than <a href="/wiki/Biopsychiatry_controversy" title="Biopsychiatry controversy">pathology or underlying cause</a>. Shell shock among frontline soldiers exposed to heavy artillery bombardment was first diagnosed by British Army doctors in 1915. By 1916, similar symptoms were also noted in soldiers not exposed to explosive shocks, leading to questions as to whether the disorder was physical or psychiatric.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> In the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Surrealism" title="Surrealism">surrealist</a> opposition to psychiatry was expressed in a number of surrealist publications.  In the 1930s several controversial medical practices were introduced including inducing seizures (by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electroshock" title="Electroshock">electroshock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> or other drugs) or cutting parts of the brain apart (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leucotomy" title="Leucotomy">leucotomy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lobotomy" title="Lobotomy">lobotomy</a>). Both came into widespread use by psychiatry, but there were grave concerns and much opposition on grounds of basic morality, harmful effects, or misuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1950s new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychiatric_drugs" title="Psychiatric drugs">psychiatric drugs</a>, notably the antipsychotic <a href="/wiki/Chlorpromazine" title="Chlorpromazine">chlorpromazine</a>, were designed in laboratories and slowly came into preferred use. Although often accepted as an advance in some ways, there was some opposition, due to serious adverse effects such as <a href="/wiki/Tardive_dyskinesia" title="Tardive dyskinesia">tardive dyskinesia</a>. Patients often opposed psychiatry and refused or stopped taking the drugs when not subject to psychiatric control. There was also increasing opposition to the use of psychiatric hospitals, and attempts to move people back into the community on a collaborative <a href="/wiki/World_Network_of_Users_and_Survivors_of_Psychiatry" title="World Network of Users and Survivors of Psychiatry">user-led group</a> approach ("therapeutic communities") not controlled by psychiatry. Campaigns against <a href="/wiki/Masturbation" title="Masturbation">masturbation</a> were done in the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> and elsewhere. <a href="/wiki/Lobotomy" title="Lobotomy">Lobotomy</a> was used until the 1970s to treat <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>. This was denounced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-psychiatric" title="Anti-psychiatric">anti-psychiatric</a> movement in the 1960s and later.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_2">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was very difficult for women to become doctors in any field before the 1970s. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Blackwell_(doctor)" title="Elizabeth Blackwell (doctor)">Elizabeth Blackwell</a> became the first woman to formally study and practice medicine in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.  She was a leader in women's medical education. While Blackwell viewed medicine as a means for social and moral reform, her student <a href="/wiki/Mary_Putnam_Jacobi" title="Mary Putnam Jacobi">Mary Putnam Jacobi</a> (1842–1906) focused on curing disease. At a deeper level of disagreement, Blackwell felt that women would succeed in medicine because of their humane female values, but Jacobi believed that women should participate as the equals of men in all medical specialties using identical methods, values and insights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> although the majority of medical doctors were women, they were paid less than the mostly male factory workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_2">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_7">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Japan</b></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doctor_(Kusakabe_Kimbei).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5376" data-file-width="7120" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg/220px-Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg/330px-Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg/440px-Doctor_%28Kusakabe_Kimbei%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doctor_(Kusakabe_Kimbei).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A doctor checks a patient's pulse in Meiji-era Japan.</div></div></div>
<p>European ideas of modern medicine were spread widely through the world by medical missionaries, and the dissemination of textbooks.  Japanese elites enthusiastically embraced Western medicine after the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of the 1860s.  However they had been prepared by their knowledge of the Dutch and German medicine, for they had some contact with Europe through the Dutch. Highly influential was the 1765 edition of Hendrik van Deventer's pioneer work <i>Nieuw Ligt</i> ("A New Light") on Japanese obstetrics, especially on Katakura Kakuryo's publication in 1799 of <i>Sanka Hatsumo</i> ("Enlightenment of Obstetrics").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup>   A cadre of Japanese physicians began to interact with Dutch doctors, who introduced smallpox vaccinations. By 1820 Japanese ranpô medical practitioners not only translated Dutch medical texts, they integrated their readings with clinical diagnoses.  These men became leaders of the modernization of medicine in their country. They broke from Japanese traditions of closed medical fraternities and adopted the European approach of an open community of collaboration based on expertise in the latest scientific methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kitasato_Shibasabur%C5%8D" title="Kitasato Shibasaburō">Kitasato Shibasaburō</a>  (1853–1931) studied bacteriology in Germany under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>. In 1891 he founded the Institute of Infectious Diseases in Tokyo, which introduced the study of bacteriology to Japan. He and French researcher <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Yersin" title="Alexandre Yersin">Alexandre Yersin</a> went to Hong Kong in 1894, where; Kitasato confirmed Yersin's discovery that the bacterium <i>Yersinia pestis</i> is the agent of the plague.  In 1897 he isolated and described the organism that caused dysentery.  He became the first dean of medicine at Keio University, and the first president of the Japan Medical Association.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>Japanese physicians immediately recognized the values of X-Rays. They were able to purchase the equipment locally from the Shimadzu Company, which developed, manufactured, marketed, and distributed X-Ray machines after 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>  Japan not only adopted German methods of public health in the home islands, but implemented them in its colonies, especially Korea and Taiwan, and after 1931 in Manchuria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup>  A heavy investment in sanitation resulted in a dramatic increase of life expectancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_5">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The practice of medicine changed in the face of rapid advances in science, as well as new approaches by physicians. Hospital doctors began much more systematic analysis of patients' symptoms in diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup> Among the more powerful new techniques were anaesthesia, and the development of both antiseptic and aseptic operating theatres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> Effective cures were developed for certain endemic infectious diseases. However, the decline in many of the most lethal diseases was due more to improvements in public health and nutrition than to advances in medicine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>Medicine was revolutionized in the 19th century and beyond by advances in chemistry, laboratory techniques, and equipment. Old ideas of infectious disease <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiology</a> were gradually replaced by advances in <a href="/wiki/Bacteriology" title="Bacteriology">bacteriology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virology" title="Virology">virology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brock_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brock-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Russian Orthodox Church sponsored seven orders of nursing sisters in the late 19th century.
</p><p>They ran hospitals, clinics, almshouses, pharmacies, and shelters as well as training schools for nurses. In the Soviet era (1917–1991), with the aristocratic sponsors gone, nursing became a low-prestige occupation based in poorly maintained hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_8">Places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>France</b>
</p><p>Paris (France) and Vienna were the two leading medical centers on the Continent in the era 1750–1914.
</p><p>In the 1770s–1850s Paris became a world center of medical research and teaching. The "Paris School" emphasized that teaching and research should be based in large hospitals and promoted the professionalization of the medical profession and the emphasis on sanitation and public health. A major reformer was <a href="/wiki/Jean-Antoine_Chaptal" title="Jean-Antoine Chaptal">Jean-Antoine Chaptal</a> (1756–1832), a physician who was Minister of Internal Affairs. He created the Paris Hospital, health councils, and other bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> (1822–1895) was one of the most important founders of <a href="/wiki/Medical_microbiology" title="Medical microbiology">medical microbiology</a>. He is remembered for his remarkable breakthroughs in the causes and preventions of diseases. His discoveries reduced mortality from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puerperal_fever" title="Puerperal fever">puerperal fever</a>, and he created the first <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> for <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>. His experiments supported the <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory of disease</a>. He was best known to the general public for inventing a method to treat milk and wine in order to prevent it from causing sickness, a process that came to be called <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a>. He is regarded as one of the three main founders of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Cohn" title="Ferdinand Cohn">Ferdinand Cohn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>.  He worked chiefly in Paris and in 1887 founded the <a href="/wiki/Pasteur_Institute" title="Pasteur Institute">Pasteur Institute</a> there to perpetuate his commitment to basic research and its practical applications. As soon as his institute was created, Pasteur brought together scientists with various specialties. The first five departments were directed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Duclaux" title="Emile Duclaux">Emile Duclaux</a> (general <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a> research) and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Chamberland" title="Charles Chamberland">Charles Chamberland</a> (microbe research applied to <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>), as well as a biologist, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilya_Ilyich_Mechnikov" title="Ilya Ilyich Mechnikov">Ilya Ilyich Mechnikov</a> (morphological microbe research) and two <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Joseph_Grancher" title="Jacques-Joseph Grancher">Jacques-Joseph Grancher</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Roux" title="Emile Roux">Emile Roux</a> (technical microbe research). One year after the inauguration of the Institut Pasteur, Roux set up the first course of microbiology ever taught in the world, then entitled <i>Cours de Microbie Technique</i> (Course of microbe research techniques). It became the model for numerous research centers around the world named "Pasteur Institutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weindling_1992_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weindling_1992-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Vienna</b>
</p><p>The First Viennese School of Medicine, 1750–1800, was led by the Dutchman <a href="/wiki/Gerard_van_Swieten" title="Gerard van Swieten">Gerard van Swieten</a> (1700–1772), who aimed to put medicine on new scientific foundations—promoting unprejudiced clinical observation, botanical and chemical research, and introducing simple but powerful remedies. When the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_General_Hospital" title="Vienna General Hospital">Vienna General Hospital</a> opened in 1784, it at once became the world's largest hospital and physicians acquired a facility that gradually developed into the most important research centre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup>  Progress ended with the Napoleonic wars and the government shutdown in 1819 of all liberal journals and schools; this caused a general return to traditionalism and eclecticism in medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vienna was the capital of a diverse empire and attracted not just Germans but Czechs, Hungarians, Jews, Poles and others to its world-class medical facilities.  After 1820 the Second Viennese School of Medicine emerged with the contributions of physicians such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carl_Freiherr_von_Rokitansky" title="Carl Freiherr von Rokitansky">Carl Freiherr von Rokitansky</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Josef_%C5%A0koda" title="Josef Škoda">Josef Škoda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Ritter_von_Hebra" title="Ferdinand Ritter von Hebra">Ferdinand Ritter von Hebra</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ignaz_Philipp_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Philipp Semmelweis">Ignaz Philipp Semmelweis</a>. Basic medical science expanded and specialization advanced. Furthermore, the first <a href="/wiki/Dermatology" title="Dermatology">dermatology</a>, eye, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ear,_nose,_and_throat" title="Ear, nose, and throat">ear, nose, and throat</a> clinics in the world were founded in Vienna.  The textbook of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ophthalmologist" title="Ophthalmologist">ophthalmologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Georg_Joseph_Beer" title="Georg Joseph Beer">Georg Joseph Beer</a> (1763–1821) <i>Lehre von den Augenkrankheiten</i> combined practical research and philosophical speculations, and became the standard reference work for decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Berlin</b>
</p><p>After 1871 Berlin, the capital of the new German Empire, became a leading center for medical research. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> (1843–1910) was a representative leader.  He became famous for isolating <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i> (1877), the <i><a href="/wiki/Mycobacterium_tuberculosis" title="Mycobacterium tuberculosis">Tuberculosis bacillus</a></i> (1882) and <i><a href="/wiki/Vibrio_cholerae" title="Vibrio cholerae">Vibrio cholerae</a></i> (1883) and for his development of <a href="/wiki/Koch%27s_postulates" title="Koch's postulates">Koch's postulates</a>. He was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a> in 1905 for his tuberculosis findings. Koch is one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>, inspiring such major figures as <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ehrlich" title="Paul Ehrlich">Paul Ehrlich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Domagk" title="Gerhard Domagk">Gerhard Domagk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weindling_1992_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weindling_1992-185">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Events_2">Events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>American Civil War</b> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hospital_Gettysburg.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hospital_Gettysburg.png/220px-Hospital_Gettysburg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hospital_Gettysburg.png/330px-Hospital_Gettysburg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Hospital_Gettysburg.png/440px-Hospital_Gettysburg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hospital_Gettysburg.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> hospital at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Gettysburg</a>, 1863</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–65), as was typical of the 19th century, more soldiers died of disease than in battle, and even larger numbers were temporarily incapacitated by wounds, disease and accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup>  Conditions were poor in the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a>, where doctors and medical supplies were in short supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> The war had a dramatic long-term impact on medicine in the U.S., from surgical technique to hospitals to nursing and to research facilities.  Weapon development -particularly the appearance of  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rifles" title="Rifles">Springfield Model 1861</a>, mass-produced and much more accurate than muskets led to generals underestimating the risks of long range rifle fire; risks exemplified in the death of <a href="/wiki/John_Sedgwick" title="John Sedgwick">John Sedgwick</a> and the disastrous <a href="/wiki/George_Pickett#Gettysburg_and_Pickett's_Charge" title="George Pickett">Pickett's Charge</a>.  The rifles could shatter bone forcing amputation and longer ranges meant casualties were sometimes not quickly found.  Evacuation of the wounded from <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Bull_Run" title="Second Battle of Bull Run">Second Battle of Bull Run</a> took a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnk_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnk-192">[191]</a></sup> As in earlier wars, untreated casualties sometimes survived unexpectedly due to maggots <a href="/wiki/Debridement" title="Debridement">debriding</a> the wound -an observation which led to the <a href="/wiki/Maggot_therapy#History" title="Maggot therapy">surgical use of maggots</a> -still a useful method in the absence of effective antibiotics.
</p><p>The hygiene of the training and field camps was poor, especially at the beginning of the war when men who had seldom been far from home were brought together for training with thousands of strangers. First came epidemics of the childhood diseases of chicken pox, mumps, whooping cough, and, especially, measles. Operations in the South meant a dangerous and new disease environment, bringing diarrhea, dysentery, typhoid fever, and malaria. There were no antibiotics, so the surgeons prescribed coffee, whiskey, and quinine. Harsh weather, bad water, inadequate shelter in winter quarters, poor policing of camps, and dirty camp hospitals took their toll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>This was a common scenario in wars from time immemorial, and conditions faced by the Confederate army were even worse. The Union responded by building army hospitals in every state. What was different in the Union was the emergence of skilled, well-funded medical organizers who took proactive action, especially in the much enlarged United States Army Medical Department,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Sanitary_Commission" title="United States Sanitary Commission">United States Sanitary Commission</a>, a new private agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> Numerous other new agencies also targeted the medical and morale needs of soldiers, including the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Christian_Commission" title="United States Christian Commission">United States Christian Commission</a> as well as smaller private agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.S. Army learned many lessons and in August 1886, it established the Hospital Corps.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_3">Institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Hospital" title="Johns Hopkins Hospital">Johns Hopkins Hospital</a>, founded in 1889, originated several modern medical practices, including <a href="/wiki/Residency_(medicine)" title="Residency (medicine)">residency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_rounds" title="Grand rounds">rounds</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="People_5">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 88.666666666667px"><div style="width: 88.666666666667px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 86.666666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur,_foto_av_Paul_Nadar,_Crisco_edit.jpg" title="Louis Pasteur (1822-1895) known for their modern founding of bacteriology, contributions to germ theory, and pasteurization."><img alt="Louis Pasteur (1822-1895) known for their modern founding of bacteriology, contributions to germ theory, and pasteurization." data-file-height="3292" data-file-width="2388" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg/130px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg/196px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg/261px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_foto_av_Paul_Nadar%2C_Crisco_edit.jpg 2x" width="87"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> (1822-1895) known for their modern founding of <a href="/wiki/Bacteriology" title="Bacteriology">bacteriology</a>, contributions to <a href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_disease" title="Germ theory of disease">germ theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a>.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 94px"><div style="width: 94px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 92px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rebecca-Lee-Crumpler.jpg" title="Rebecca Lee Crumpler (1831-1895) known as the first African-American woman to become a physician."><img alt="Rebecca Lee Crumpler (1831-1895) known as the first African-American woman to become a physician." data-file-height="256" data-file-width="197" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Rebecca-Lee-Crumpler.jpg/138px-Rebecca-Lee-Crumpler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Rebecca-Lee-Crumpler.jpg 1.5x" width="92"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Rebecca_Lee_Crumpler" title="Rebecca Lee Crumpler">Rebecca Lee Crumpler</a> (1831-1895) known as the first <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African-American</a> woman to become a physician.
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_medicine">20th century medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: 20th century medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular">Cardiovascular</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Cardiovascular">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_groups">Blood groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Blood groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/ABO_blood_group_system" title="ABO blood group system">ABO blood group system</a> was discovered in 1901 by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Landsteiner" title="Karl Landsteiner">Karl Landsteiner</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>. <a href="/wiki/Karl_Landsteiner" title="Karl Landsteiner">Landsteiner</a> experimented on his staff, mixing their various blood components together, and found that some people's blood agglutinated (clumped together) with other blood, whilst some did not. This then lead him identifying three blood groups, ABC, which would later be renamed to ABO.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> The less frequently found blood group <a href="/wiki/ABO_blood_group_system" title="ABO blood group system">AB</a> was discovered later in 1902 by Alfred Von Decastello and Adriano Sturli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Legese_2021_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Legese_2021-198">[197]</a></sup> In 1937 <a href="/wiki/Karl_Landsteiner" title="Karl Landsteiner">Landsteiner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_S._Wiener" title="Alexander S. Wiener">Alexander S. Wiener</a> further discovered the Rh factor (misnamed from early thinking that this blood group was similar to that found in <a href="/wiki/Rhesus_macaque" title="Rhesus macaque">rhesus monkies</a>) whose antigens further determine blood reaction between people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Legese_2021_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Legese_2021-198">[197]</a></sup> This was demonstrated in a 1939 case study by Phillip Levine and Rufus Stetson where a mother who had recently given birth had reacted to their partner's blood, highlighting the Rh factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_transfusion">Blood transfusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Blood transfusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Canadian physician <a href="/wiki/Norman_Bethune" title="Norman Bethune">Norman Bethune</a>, M.D. developed a mobile <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood-transfusion</a> service for frontline operations in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> (1936–1939), but ironically, he himself died of <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pacemaker">Pacemaker</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Pacemaker">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1958, <a href="/wiki/Arne_Larsson_(patient)" title="Arne Larsson (patient)">Arne Larsson</a> in Sweden became the first patient to depend on an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_cardiac_pacemaker" title="Artificial cardiac pacemaker">artificial cardiac pacemaker</a>. He died in 2001 at age 86, having outlived its inventor, the surgeon, and 26 pacemakers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cancer_treatment" title="Cancer treatment">Cancer treatment</a> has been developed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiotherapy" title="Radiotherapy">radiotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surgical_oncology" title="Surgical oncology">surgical oncology</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_radiography" title="Medical radiography">X-ray imaging</a> was the first kind of <a href="/wiki/Medical_imaging" title="Medical imaging">medical imaging</a>, and later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultrasonic_imaging" title="Ultrasonic imaging">ultrasonic imaging</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CT_scanning" title="CT scanning">CT scanning</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MR_scanning" title="MR scanning">MR scanning</a> and other imaging methods became available.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disabilities">Disabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Disabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg/220px-Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg/330px-Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg/440px-Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Infant_with_cochlear_implant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Cochlear_implant" title="Cochlear implant">cochlear implant</a> is a common kind of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_prosthesis" title="Neural prosthesis">neural prosthesis</a>, a device replacing part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_nervous_system" title="Human nervous system">human nervous system</a>.</div></div></div><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosthetic" title="Prosthetic">Prosthetics</a> have improved with lightweight materials as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_prosthetic" title="Neural prosthetic">neural prosthetics</a> emerging in the end of the 20th century.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diseases">Diseases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Diseases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_therapy" title="Oral rehydration therapy">Oral rehydration therapy</a> has been extensively used since the 1970s to treat <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> and other diarrhea-inducing infections.
</p><p>As infectious diseases have become less lethal, and the most common <a href="/wiki/Cause_of_death" title="Cause of death">causes of death</a> in developed countries are now <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular diseases</a>, these conditions have received increased attention in medical research.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disease_eradication">Disease eradication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Disease eradication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaria_eradication">Malaria eradication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Malaria eradication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_malaria" title="History of malaria">History of malaria</a></div>
<p>Starting in World War II, <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> was used as insecticide to combat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insect_vector" title="Insect vector">insect vectors</a> carrying <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, which was endemic in most tropical regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup> The first goal was to protect soldiers, but it was widely adopted as a public health device.  In Liberia, for example, the United States had large military operations during the war and the U.S. Public Health Service began the use of DDT for indoor residual spraying (IRS) and as a larvicide, with the goal of controlling malaria in Monrovia, the Liberian capital. In the early 1950s, the project was expanded to nearby villages. In 1953, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) launched an antimalaria program in parts of Liberia as a pilot project to determine the feasibility of malaria eradication in tropical Africa. However these projects encountered a spate of difficulties that foreshadowed the general retreat from malaria eradication efforts across tropical Africa by the mid-1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb_2011_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb_2011-204">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pandemics">Pandemics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Pandemics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="1918_influenza_pandemic_.281918-1920.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1918_influenza_pandemic_(1918-1920)">1918 influenza pandemic (1918-1920)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: 1918 influenza pandemic (1918-1920)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">1918 influenza pandemic</a> was a global pandemic in the early 20th century that occurred between 1918 to 1920. Sometimes known as Spanish Flu due to popular opinion at the time thinking the flu originated from Spain, this pandemic caused close to 50 million deaths around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> Spreading at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health">Public health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: Public health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">Public health</a> measures became particularly important during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a>, which killed at least 50 million people around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> It became an <a href="/wiki/Spanish_flu_research" title="Spanish flu research">important case study in epidemiology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> Bristow shows there was a gendered response of health caregivers to the pandemic in the United States.  Male doctors were unable to cure the patients, and they felt like failures.  Women nurses also saw their patients die, but they took pride in their success in fulfilling their professional role of caring for, ministering, comforting, and easing the last hours of their patients, and helping the families of the patients cope as well.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Evidence-based_medicine" title="Evidence-based medicine">Evidence-based medicine</a> is a modern concept, not introduced to literature until the 1990s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_and_reproductive_health">Sexual and reproductive health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=60" title="Edit section: Sexual and reproductive health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="335" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg/220px-Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg/330px-Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg/440px-Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Life_expectancy_in_select_Southern_African_countries_1960-2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most countries have seen a tremendous increase in life expectancy since 1945. However, in southern Africa, the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> epidemic beginning around 1990 has eroded national health.</div></div></div><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sexual_revolution" title="Sexual revolution">sexual revolution</a> included taboo-breaking research in <a href="/wiki/Human_sexuality" title="Human sexuality">human sexuality</a> such as the 1948 and 1953 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinsey_reports" title="Kinsey reports">Kinsey reports</a>, invention of <a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">hormonal contraception</a>, and the normalization of <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> in many countries. <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a> has promoted a <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a> in most of the world. With threatening <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_transmitted_infections" title="Sexually transmitted infections">sexually transmitted infections</a>, not least <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>, use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_contraception" title="Barrier contraception">barrier contraception</a> has become imperative. The struggle against HIV has improved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiretroviral" title="Antiretroviral">antiretroviral</a> treatments.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smoking">Smoking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=61" title="Edit section: Smoking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">Tobacco smoking</a> as a cause of <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> was first researched in the 1920s, but was not widely supported by publications until the 1950s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surgery">Surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=62" title="Edit section: Surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cardiac_surgery" title="Cardiac surgery">Cardiac surgery</a> was revolutionized in 1948 as open-heart surgery was introduced for the first time since 1925. In 1954 <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Murray" title="Joseph Murray">Joseph Murray</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._Hartwell_Harrison" title="J. Hartwell Harrison">J. Hartwell Harrison</a> and others accomplished the first <a href="/wiki/Kidney_transplantation" title="Kidney transplantation">kidney transplantation</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplant" title="Organ transplant">Transplantations</a> of other organs, such as heart, liver and pancreas, were also introduced during the later 20th century. The first partial <a href="/wiki/Face_transplant" title="Face transplant">face transplant</a> was performed in 2005, and the first full one in 2010. By the end of the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Microtechnology" title="Microtechnology">microtechnology</a> had been used to create tiny robotic devices to assist <a href="/wiki/Microsurgery" title="Microsurgery">microsurgery</a> using micro-video and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiber_optics" title="Fiber optics">fiber-optic</a> cameras to view internal tissues during surgery with minimally invasive practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15631913_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15631913-209">[208]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laparoscopic_surgery" title="Laparoscopic surgery">Laparoscopic surgery</a> was broadly introduced in the 1990s. Natural orifice surgery has followed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War">War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=63" title="Edit section: War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Mexican_Revolution_.281910-1920.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexican_Revolution_(1910-1920)">Mexican Revolution (1910-1920)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=64" title="Edit section: Mexican Revolution (1910-1920)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 19th century, large-scale wars were attended with <a href="/wiki/Medic" title="Medic">medics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mobile_hospital" title="Mobile hospital">mobile hospital</a> units which developed advanced techniques for healing massive injuries and controlling infections rampant in battlefield conditions. During the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a> (1910–1920), General <a href="/wiki/Pancho_Villa" title="Pancho Villa">Pancho Villa</a> organized hospital trains for wounded soldiers. Boxcars marked <i>Servicio Sanitario</i> ("sanitary service") were re-purposed as surgical operating theaters and areas for recuperation, and staffed by up to 40 Mexican and U.S. physicians. Severely wounded soldiers were shuttled back to base hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="World_War_I_.281914-1918.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_(1914-1918)">World War I (1914-1918)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: World War I (1914-1918)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg/220px-Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg/330px-Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg/440px-Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medicine_during_the_First_World_War-_Medical_Transport_Q33419.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Medicine during the First World War - Medical Transport.</div></div></div><p>Thousands of scarred troops provided the need for improved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prosthetic_limb" title="Prosthetic limb">prosthetic limbs</a> and expanded techniques in plastic surgery or <a href="/wiki/Reconstructive_surgery" title="Reconstructive surgery">reconstructive surgery</a>. Those practices were combined to broaden <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmetic_surgery" title="Cosmetic surgery">cosmetic surgery</a> and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Elective_surgery" title="Elective surgery">elective surgery</a>.
</p><h4><span id="Interwar_period_.281918-1939.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Interwar_period_(1918-1939)">Interwar period (1918-1939)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Interwar period (1918-1939)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>From 1917 to 1932, the <a href="/wiki/American_Red_Cross" title="American Red Cross">American Red Cross</a> moved into Europe with a battery of long-term child health projects.  It built and operated hospitals and clinics, and organized antituberculosis and antityphus campaigns.  A high priority involved child health programs such as clinics, better baby shows, playgrounds, fresh air camps, and courses for women on infant hygiene. Hundreds of U.S. doctors, nurses, and welfare professionals administered these programs, which aimed to reform the health of European youth and to reshape European public health and welfare along American lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22643983_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22643983-212">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Word_War_II_.281939-1945.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_War_II_(1939-1945)">Word War II (1939-1945)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Word War II (1939-1945)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ww2-53.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1097" data-file-width="1349" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ww2-53.jpg/220px-Ww2-53.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ww2-53.jpg/330px-Ww2-53.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Ww2-53.jpg/440px-Ww2-53.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ww2-53.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Combat_surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Combat surgery (page does not exist)">combat surgery</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">Pacific War</a>, 1943. Major wars showed the need for effective hygiene and medical treatment.</div></div></div><p>The advances in medicine made a dramatic difference for Allied troops, while the Germans and especially the Japanese and Chinese suffered from a severe lack of newer medicines, techniques and facilities.  Harrison finds that the chances of recovery for a badly wounded British infantryman were as much as 25 times better than in the First World War. The reason was that:
</p><dl><dd>"By 1944 most casualties were receiving treatment within hours of wounding, due to the increased mobility of field hospitals and the extensive use of aeroplanes as ambulances. The care of the sick and wounded had also been revolutionized by new medical technologies, such as active immunization against tetanus, sulphonamide drugs, and penicillin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">second World War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Carrel" title="Alexis Carrel">Alexis Carrel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Drysdale_Dakin" title="Henry Drysdale Dakin">Henry Dakin</a> developed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carrel-Dakin_method" title="Carrel-Dakin method">Carrel-Dakin method</a> of treating wounds with an irrigation, Dakin's solution, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germicide" title="Germicide">germicide</a> which helped prevent <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup>
</p><p>The War spurred the usage of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm Röntgen">Roentgen</a>'s <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrocardiograph" title="Electrocardiograph">electrocardiograph</a>, for the <a href="/wiki/Monitoring_(medicine)" title="Monitoring (medicine)">monitoring</a> of internal bodily functions.  This was followed in the inter-war period by the development of the first anti-bacterial agents such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulpha" title="Sulpha">sulpha</a> antibiotics.
</p><p><b>Nazi and Japanese medical research</b>
</p><p>Unethical <a href="/wiki/Human_subject_research" title="Human subject research">human subject research</a>, and killing of patients with disabilities, peaked during the Nazi era, with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_human_experimentation" title="Nazi human experimentation">Nazi human experimentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aktion_T4" title="Aktion T4">Aktion T4</a> during <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> as the most significant examples. Many of the details of these and related events were the focus of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctors%27_Trial" title="Doctors' Trial">Doctors' Trial</a>. Subsequently, principles of <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">medical ethics</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Code" title="Nuremberg Code">Nuremberg Code</a>, were introduced to prevent a recurrence of such atrocities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup>  After 1937, the Japanese Army established programs of biological warfare in China. In <a href="/wiki/Unit_731" title="Unit 731">Unit 731</a>, Japanese doctors and research scientists conducted large numbers of vivisections and experiments on human beings, mostly Chinese victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutions_4">Institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Health_Organization">World Health Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: World Health Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger,_1969.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2750" data-file-width="2124" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg/170px-Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg/255px-Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg/340px-Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger%2C_1969.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fighting_smallpox_in_Niger,_1969.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">Smallpox</a> vaccination in Niger, 1969. A decade later, this was the first infectious disease to be eradicated.</div></div></div><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> was founded in 1948 as a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> agency to improve <a href="/wiki/Global_health" title="Global health">global health</a>. In most of the world, <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> has improved since then, and was about 67 years as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, and well above 80 years in some countries. <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">Eradication of infectious diseases</a> is an international effort, and several new <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> have been developed during the post-war years, against infections such as <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumps" title="Mumps">mumps</a>, several strains of <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_papilloma_virus" title="Human papilloma virus">human papilloma virus</a>. The long-known vaccine against <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">Smallpox</a> finally eradicated the disease in the 1970s, and <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">Rinderpest</a> was wiped out in 2011. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eradication_of_polio" title="Eradication of polio">Eradication of polio</a> is underway. <a href="/wiki/Tissue_culture" title="Tissue culture">Tissue culture</a> is important for development of vaccines. Though the early success of antiviral vaccines and antibacterial drugs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiviral_drugs" title="Antiviral drugs">antiviral drugs</a> were not introduced until the 1970s. Through the WHO, the international community has developed a response protocol against epidemics, displayed during the <a href="/wiki/SARS" title="SARS">SARS</a> epidemic in 2003, the <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H5N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H5N1">Influenza A virus subtype H5N1</a> from 2004, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_West_Africa" title="Ebola virus epidemic in West Africa">Ebola virus epidemic in West Africa</a> and onwards.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="People_6">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_medicine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
